Divine Attributes

Second Pole: The Seven Attributes of God
Ghazali introduces the second pole, lists seven attributes and states their essential properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of God as outlined by Imam al-Ghazali and their three key properties: being additional to the essence, eternal, and subsisting in the essence.

Core Idea
God's seven attributes are real, eternal, and distinct from His essence.

Imam al-Ghazali now begins the second pole of his creed, which focuses on God’s attributes. He lists seven attributes — life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and speech — and immediately clarifies their relationship to the divine essence. This passage establishes the foundational framework for understanding how classical Ash‘ari theology conceives of God’s attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure: He is living, knowing... and He has life, knowledge...
2
Identify the seven attributes and their nouns
Find each of the seven attribute names (حي, عالم, etc.) and their corresponding nouns (حياة, علم, etc.). Write them down.
This is a classic enumeration common in kalam works.
3
Understand the three properties
The passage states three properties of these attributes: they are additional to the essence (زائدة على الذات), eternal (قديمة), and subsisting in the essence (قائمة بالذات). Reflect on what each means.
These properties distinguish the Ash'ari position from others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطب الثاني : في صفات الله تعالى : ونبيِّنُ فيه أنَّه حيٌّ ، عالم ، قادر ، مريد ، سميع ، بصير ، متكلم وأنَّ له حياةً ، وعلماً ، وقدرة ، وإرادة ، وسمعاً ، وبصراً ، وكلاماً . ونذكرُ أحكام هذه الصفات ولوازمها ، وما يفترقُ فيها وما يجتمع فيها من الأحكام ، وأنَّ هذه الصفات زائدة على الذات ، وقديمة وقائمة بالذات ، ولا يجوز أن يكون شيء من الصفات حادثاً
The second pole: On the attributes of God, exalted. We will explain in it that He is living, knowing, powerful, willing, hearing, seeing, speaking. And that He has life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech. And we will mention the rulings of these attributes and their implications, and what differs and what agrees among them in rulings, and that these attributes are additional to the essence, eternal, and subsisting in the essence, and it is not permissible for any of the attributes to be originated.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage describes the second pole, which treats God's attributes. Ghazali lists seven attributes: life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and speech. He states that these attributes are additional to the essence (za'ida 'ala al-dhat), eternal (qadima), and subsisting in the essence (qa'ima bi al-dhat). He will examine their rulings and implications, noting differences and commonalities. A key point is that none of these attributes can be originated (hadith); they are eternal. This is a standard Ash'ari position asserting real, eternal attributes distinct from the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the attributes are identical to the essence or that they are separate entities.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that they are additional to the essence (za'ida 'ala al-dhat) but not separate; they subsist in the essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the status of the attributes in relation to God's essence?
A) They are identical to the essence
B) They are additional to the essence
C) They are created
D) They are temporal

Key Takeaway
God’s attributes are seven, real, eternal, and distinct from His essence yet not separate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is All-Knowing,' do you really believe His knowledge is eternal—meaning He has always known every detail of your life, including your secret thoughts? How does that change the way you speak to Him today?
?
The attributes are said to be 'additional to the essence' but not separate. Does your concept of God sometimes treat His mercy or justice as if they are separate 'parts'? How can you worship Him as a unified Being with real, distinct qualities?
?
If you truly internalized that God's speech (kalam) is eternal, would you treat the Quran as a mere book or as the very words of the Eternal One? What would you change in your relationship with the Quran starting today?

The Non-Transferability of Accidents
Ghazali argues that accidents are essentially tied to their subjects and cannot transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that accidents cannot transfer from one subject to another, because their specificity to a subject is essential to their existence.

Core Idea
An accident has no existence apart from its subject; transference would annihilate it.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a crucial argument about the nature of accidents. He uses the example of length in a person to show that accidents are intelligible only in relation to their subjects. This leads to the conclusion that the world is never devoid of originated events, a key principle in his proof for the world's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the contrast between how length (an accident) and a substance are understood.
Pay attention to the terms 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) and 'substance' (jawhar).
2
Understand the concept of 'essential specificity'
Ghazali explains that the accident's connection to its subject is not added but essential (dhātī). This means that if the connection is severed, the accident ceases to exist.
Contrast this with a substance, whose connection to a location is additional.
3
Trace the argument against transference
Follow why transference would nullify the accident's essence. The argument hinges on the idea that the accident's essence is nothing other than being for that specific subject.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
Ghazali uses this to establish that the world is never free from originated events (motion and rest), which are accidents and non-transferable. This supports his proof for the world's creation.
Even philosophers agree that bodies are always in motion or rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس كذلك طول زيد مثلاً؛ فإنه عَرَضُ في زيد لا نعقلُه في نفسه دون زيد؛ بل نعقل زيداً الطويل، فطولُ زيد يعلم تابعاً لوجود زيد، ويلزم من تقدير عدم زيد بطلان طول زيد، فليس لطول زيد قوام في الوجود وفي العقل دون زيد، فاختصاصه بزيد ذاتي له؛ أي: هو لذاته، لا لمعنى زائد عليه هو اختصاص، فإن بطل ذلك الاختصاص... بطلت ذاته! والانتقالُ يُبطِلُ الاختصاص، فتبطل ذاتُه؛ إذ ليس اختصاصه بزيد زائداً على ذاته؛ أعني ذات العرض، بخلاف اختصاص الجوهر بالحيّز؛ فإنَّه زائد عليه، فليس في بطلانه بالانتقال ما يبطل ذاته، ورجع الكلام إلى أن الانتقال يبطل الاختصاص بالمحل، فإن كان الاختصاص بالمحل زائداً على الذات لم تبطل به الذات، وإن لم يكن معنى زائداً بطل ببطلانه الذات. فقد انكشف هذا، وآل النظر إلى أنَّ اختصاص العرض بمحله لم يكن زائداً على ذات العرض كاختصاص الجوهر بحيزه، وذلك لما ذكرناه من أن الجوهر عُقِلَ وحدَهُ، وعُقِلَ الحيز به، لا أنَّ الجوهر عُقِلَ بالحيز. وأما العرض: فإنّما عُقِلَ بالجوهر لا بنفسه، فذاتُ العرض هو كونه للجوهر المعيَّن، وليس له ذات سواه، فإذا قُدِّرَ مفارقته لذلك الجوهر المعيَّن فقد قُدَّر عدم ذاته، وإنما فرضنا الكلام في الطول ليفهم المقصود، فإنَّهُ وإنْ لم يكن عرضاً ولكنه عبارةٌ عن كثرة الأجسام في جهة واحدة فهو مقرّب لغرضنا إلى الفهم، فإذا فهم فلننقل البيان إلى الأعراض. وهذا التدقيق والتحقيق وإن لم يكن لائقاً بهذا الإيجاز ولكن افتقر إليه؛ لأن ما ذكر فيه غير مقنع ولا شاف. فقد فرغنا عن إثبات أحد الأصلين، وهو أنَّ العالم لا يخلو عن الحوادث؛ فإنَّه لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، وهما حادثان وليسا بمنتقلين، مع أن هذا الإطناب ليس في مقابلة خصم معتقد؛ إذ أجمعَ الفلاسفة على أن أجسام العالم لا تخلو عن الحوادث، وهم المنكرون لحدث العالم.
But the length of Zayd, for example, is not like that; for it is an accident in Zayd, not intelligible in itself apart from Zayd; rather we understand the tall Zayd. So the length of Zayd is known as a consequence of Zayd's existence, and from assuming Zayd's non-existence follows the nullification of Zayd's length. So the length of Zayd has no subsistence in existence or intellect apart from Zayd. Thus its specificity to Zayd is essential to it; i.e., it is by itself, not by an additional meaning that is specificity. So if that specificity is nullified, its essence is nullified! And transference nullifies specificity, so its essence is nullified, since its specificity to Zayd is not additional to its essence; I mean the essence of the accident, unlike the substance's specificity to a location; for that is additional to it, so its nullification by transference does not nullify its essence. The argument returns to the fact that transference nullifies specificity to the subject. If the specificity to the subject is additional to the essence, the essence is not nullified by it; if it is not an additional meaning, the essence is nullified by its nullification. This has now been clarified, and the inquiry leads to the conclusion that the accident's specificity to its subject is not additional to the accident's essence, like the substance's specificity to its location, because of what we mentioned: that the substance is understood by itself, and its location is understood through it, not that the substance is understood by the location. As for the accident, it is understood only through the substance, not by itself. So the accident's essence is its being for a particular substance, and it has no essence besides that. So if its separation from that particular substance is assumed, its non-existence is assumed. We only posited the discussion about length to facilitate understanding of the intended point; for although it is not an accident, it is an expression of the multiplicity of bodies in one direction, so it brings our purpose closer to understanding. So when understood, let us transfer the explanation to accidents. This precision and verification, though not befitting this brevity, we needed it because what was mentioned regarding it is not convincing nor sufficient. So we have finished establishing one of the two principles: that the world is never free from events (hawadith), for it is never free from motion and rest, both of which are originated (hadith) and not transferable, although this prolixity is not in response to a believing opponent, for the philosophers agree that the bodies of the world are never free from events, and they are the deniers of the world's origination.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument defending the non-transferability of accidents and establishing the first principle that the world is never free from originated events. Ghazali begins by contrasting the length of Zayd with a substance. Length is an accident inhering in Zayd; it is not intelligible independently of Zayd. We understand 'the tall Zayd' as a whole, not length by itself. Therefore, length's existence depends on Zayd, and if Zayd is annihilated, so is its length. This specificity (ikhtiṣāṣ) to Zayd is essential (dhātī) to the accident—it is not an added attribute. Transference (intiqāl) would nullify that essential specificity, thus annihilating the accident. In contrast, a substance (jawhar) has specificity to a location (ḥayyiz) that is additional to its essence; if it moves, its location changes but its substance remains. So accidents cannot transfer because their very essence is tied to their subject. Ghazali then clarifies that an accident is only understood through its subject (al-ʿaraḍ innamā ʿuqila bi-l-jawhar lā bi-nafsihi), and its essence is nothing other than being for that specific substance. He uses the example of length (ṭūl) to illustrate, though length is not strictly an accident but a relation of extension; it still shows dependence on the body. After this precision, he concludes that the first principle is established: the world is never without originated events (ḥawādith), specifically motion and rest. Even philosophers who deny the world's origination admit that bodies are always in motion or rest, so this point is conceded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidents can transfer from one substance to another like a substance moves.
Correction: Ghazali shows that this would destroy the accident's essence because its specificity to its subject is essential, not accidental.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does transference (intiqal) annihilate an accident's essence?
A) Because the accident's specificity to its subject is essential to its existence.
B) Because the accident is weaker than the substance.
C) Because transference requires a new subject which the accident cannot acquire.
D) Because the accident is not real.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot transfer because their essence is nothing other than being for a specific subject; transference would nullify that essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that an accident's essence is entirely dependent on its subject. Think of a personal attribute you value deeply — your intelligence, your health, your appearance. If that attribute were stripped away, would you still know who you are? Are you treating that attribute as if it had independent existence, or do you see it as a borrowed quality from the One who sustains you? How will you remind yourself daily that your essence belongs not to these transient things but to Allah?
?
Ghazali tells us that transference would annihilate the accident's essence. If you were to 'transfer' your identity from one role or possession to another, would you lose yourself? Where have you mistakenly placed your essential identity in things that can be taken away? Commit to one concrete change today that re-anchors your sense of self in your relationship with the Eternal.

God's Infinite Power and Knowledge: Resolving the Apparent Inequality
Ghazali refutes an objection about infinite objects of power and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali resolves the objection that God's infinite objects of knowledge exceed His infinite objects of power by distinguishing between unceasing capability and an actual infinite set of things.

Core Idea
Infinite power means unceasing ability, not an infinite number of created things.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts a subtle objection: if both God's power and knowledge are infinite, but known things include God's essence and attributes which are not subject to power, then one infinite would be greater than another—a logical problem. Ghazali's answer lies in clarifying what 'infinite' means in each context. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the objection
Read the objector's statement carefully. Notice the claim: objects of knowledge include God's essence and attributes, which are not objects of power, so knowledge's object set is larger.
The objector assumes both 'infinities' refer to sets of actual things.
2
Identify Ghazali's core distinction
Ghazali says 'no end to power' means the capability (tā'ī) never ceases, not that there are infinitely many actual objects. Contrast this with 'no end to knowledge', which he hints has a deeper meaning.
This distinction avoids the inequality problem.
3
Avoid the linguistic trap
The error comes from assuming that similar Arabic morphological patterns (maf'ūlāt) imply similar meanings. Ghazali warns that the words 'maqdūrāt' and 'maʻlūmāt' share form but not meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : مقدوراتُ الله تعالى عندكم لا نهاية لها ، وكذا معلوماته لا تفاوت بين اللانهايتين والمعلومات أكثر من المقدورات ؛ إذ ذاتُ القديم وصفاته معلومةٌ له ، وكذا لا نهاية الموجود المستمر الوجود ، وليس شيء من ذلك مقدوراً ؟ قلنا : نحن إذا قلنا : لا نهاية لمقدوراته.. لم نرد به ما نريد بقولنا لا نهاية لمعلوماته ، بل نريد به : أنَّ الله تعالى صفةً يعبر عنها بالقدرة ، يُتأتّى بها الإيجاد ، وهذا التأتي لا ينعدم قط وليس تحت قولنا : ( هذا التأتي لا ينعدم ( إثبات أشياء فضلاً عن أن توصف بأنها متناهية أو غير متناهية ، وإنما يقع هذا الغلط لمن ينظر في الألفاظ فيرى توازن لفظ ( المعلومات ) و ( المقدورات ) من حيث التصريف في اللغة ، فيظن أن المراد بهما واحدا هيهات ؛ فلا مناسبة بينهما ألبتة ثم تحت قولنا : ( المعلومات لا نهاية لها ( أيضاً سرّ يُخالف السابق منه إلى الفهم ؛ إذ السابق إلى الفهم إثبات أشياء تسمى : معلوماتٍ لا نهاية ، وهو محال ، بل الأشياء هي الموجودات ، وهي متناهية ، ولكن بيان ذلك يستدعي تطويلاً
If it is said: The objects of God's power (maqdūrāt) according to you have no end, and similarly His objects of knowledge (maʻlūmāt) have no end. There is no difference between the two infinites, yet the objects of knowledge are more than the objects of power, because the essence of the Eternal and His attributes are known to Him, and likewise the infinite of perpetually existing beings, and none of that is an object of power. We say: When we say 'there is no end to His objects of power' we do not mean by it what we mean by our saying 'there is no end to His objects of knowledge'. Rather we mean by it that God—exalted is He—has an attribute expressed by 'power' by which bringing-into-existence is facilitated, and this facilitation never ceases. And under our statement 'this facilitation never ceases' there is no affirmation of things, let alone that they are described as finite or infinite. This error only occurs to one who looks at expressions and sees the parallel form of the words 'information' (al-maʻlūmāt) and 'objects of power' (al-maqdūrāt) in terms of linguistic morphology, and so supposes that both refer to one thing. Far from it! There is absolutely no correspondence between them. Then under our statement 'the objects of knowledge have no end' there is also a secret contrary to what first comes to mind. For what first comes to mind is the affirmation of things called 'objects of knowledge' without end, and that is impossible. Rather, things are existents, and they are finite. But explaining that would require a lengthy discussion.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector points out that Ghazali claims God's powers are infinite (no end) and His knowledge also infinite. Yet known things (like God's essence and attributes, and the infinite chain of existents) are more than the objects of power, since not all known are subject to power. This would mean one infinite (knowledge) is greater than another (power), which is the same absurdity Ghazali had used against infinite rotations. Ghazali's answer begins by clarifying that the phrase 'no end to His powers' does not mean the same as 'no end to His knowledge'. When he says powers are without end, he means that God's ability (al-tā'ī) to bring things into existence never ceases. This is not a statement about a quantity of actual objects; rather it refers to a continuous capability. The objector's error is ignoring the semantic difference: the words maʻlūmāt and maqdūrāt share a grammatical form but do not refer to the same kind of entity. Ghazali adds that even the statement 'objects of knowledge are infinite' has a deeper meaning that contradicts the superficial understanding: the superficial understanding posits an infinite set of known things, which is impossible; actually, existents are finite. He declines to explain fully, saying it would take too long.

Key Terms
مقدورات (objects of power)
معلومات (objects of knowledge)
لا نهاية (no end/infinite)
التأتي (facilitation/capability)
الإيجاد (bringing into existence)
غلط (error)
توازن اللفظ (parallel wording)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'infinite objects of power' means an actual infinite set of created things.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that it means the capability itself is unceasing, not that there are infinitely many actual objects of power.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali solve the apparent inequality between infinite objects of power and knowledge?
A) He denies that objects of knowledge are infinite
B) He says 'no end to power' means the capability never ceases, not an infinite set
C) He admits the contradiction and revises his position
D) He argues power and knowledge are the same

Key Takeaway
Ghazali resolves the apparent contradiction by clarifying that 'no end to power' refers to the unceasing ability to create, not to an infinite collection of actual entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that God's power is infinite, but do you live as though His power is limited by what you can see? In what area of your life are you acting as if God cannot bring about something simply because it does not yet exist? What would change if you truly believed His ability to bring things into existence never ceases?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible, do you secretly think that God's power is exhausted because the outcome is not yet realized? How does this lesson's distinction between God's unceasing capacity and the finiteness of actual created things challenge your impatience or despair? Repent of limiting God's power to your present circumstances.
?
The objector fell into error by fixating on words rather than meanings. Where in your own understanding of Islamic theology have you assumed that similar terms mean the same thing, leading you to a false conclusion? How can you train yourself to go beyond superficial linguistic parallels to grasp the deeper realities?

Resolving the Paradox of Infinite Divine Power and Knowledge
Ghazali answers an objection about infinite attributes and concludes the proof of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali resolves the apparent paradox of infinite divine attributes and how this clarification supports the proof of the Creator's existence.

Core Idea
Divine infinity is not about quantity but about the nature of power and knowledge.

Ghazali tackles a sophisticated objection: if God's power and knowledge are both infinite, how can knowledge be 'more'? He shows that the objection is based on a linguistic misunderstanding. By clarifying the intended meanings, he not only defends the doctrine but also uses the occasion to reaffirm the proof of the Creator from the world's origination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening 'تحريجة' to grasp the paradox: if the world is originated, the number of solar and lunar rotations are both infinite yet different, and similarly God's objects of power and knowledge seem to differ.
The objection tries to show a contradiction in saying divine attributes are infinite.
2
See Ghazali's clarification about power
Notice how Ghazali explains that 'no end to objects of power' means the attribute of power enables creation without cessation, not that there is an actual infinite set of objects.
This is a key shift from a quantitative to a qualitative understanding.
3
Understand the clarification about knowledge
Ghazali notes that 'no end to objects of knowledge' is also misunderstood: it does not mean an infinite set of actual things, as existents are finite; rather it refers to the comprehensiveness of divine knowledge.
The misunderstanding arises from focusing on linguistic forms rather than meanings.
4
Connect to the proof of the Creator
Ghazali concludes that once the objection is repelled, the syllogism 'the world is originated, every originated thing has a cause, so the world has a cause' stands, proving the existence of the Maker.
However, the nature and attributes of the cause require further investigation.

تحريجة: سلمنا أنه لها (۱) وهي أقل من دورات الشمس؛ إذ يعلم ضرورة: أن ثلث الشيء أقل من الشيء عشرِ والقمر يدور في السنة اثنتي عشرة مرةً، فيكون عدد دورات الشمس مثلاً نصف سدس دوراتِ القمر، وكلُّ واحد لا نهاية له، وبعضُه أقل من بعض، وذلك من المحال البينِ. فإن قيل: مقدوراتُ الله تعالى عندكم لا نهاية لها، وكذا معلوماته لا تفاوت بين والمعلومات أكثر من المقدورات؛ إذ ذاتُ القديم وصفاته معلومةٌ له، وكذا اللانهايتين فكيف تفاوتت لا نهاية الموجود المستمر الوجود، وليس شيء من ذلك مقدوراً؟(۲) القدرة مع لانهاية العلم؟ التحريجة. قلنا: نحن إذا قلنا: لا نهاية لمقدوراته.. لم نرد به ما نريد بقولنا لا نهاية لمعلوماته، بل نريد به: أنَّ الله تعالى صفةً يعبر عنها بالقدرة، يُتأتّى بها الإيجاد (۳)، وهذا التأتي لا ينعدم قط (٤) وليس تحت قولنا: ( هذا التأتي لا ينعدم ( إثبات أشياء فضلاً عن أن المعاني من منشا الغلط لهذ. توصف بأنها متناهية أو غير متناهية، وإنما يقع هذا الغلط لمن ينظر في الألفاظ فيرى توازن لفظ ( المعلومات ) و ( المقدورات ) من حيث التصريف في اللغة، فيظن أن المراد بهما واحدا هيهات؛ فلا مناسبة بينهما ألبتة. ثم تحت قولنا: ( المعلومات لا نهاية لها ( أيضاً سرّ يُخالف السابق منه إلى الفهم؛ إذ السابق إلى الفهم إثبات أشياء تسمى: معلوماتٍ لا نهاية، وهو محال، بل الأشياء هي الموجودات، وهي متناهية، ولكن بيان لها ذلك يستدعي تطويلاً (1) على ما يراه القائل بقدم العالم (۲) فيلزم من ذلك تفاوت اللامتناهيين؟ ويمكن صياغة اعتراضهم: إن علمه متعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل، بخلاف القدرة المتعلقة بالجائز فقط (۳) ولم يقل: يُتأتى بها الإيجاد والإعدام؛ للخلاف في تعلق القدرة بإعدام الممكن بعد وجوده (٤) فعبرنا عن استحالة انعدام هذا التأتي باللانهاية للقدرة . وقد اندفعَ الإشكال بالكشف عن معنى نفي النهاية عن المقدورات، فالنظرُ في الطرف الثاني - وهو المعلومات - مستغنى عنه في دفع الإلزام فقد بانت صحة هذا الأصل بالمنهج الثالث من مناهج الأدلة المذكورة في التمهيد الرابع من الكتاب وعند هذا يُعلمُ وجود الصانع؛ إذ بانَ القياس الذي ذكرناه؛ وهو قولنا: إنَّ العالم حادث، وكلُّ حادث فله سببٌ؛ فالعالم له سبب فقد ثبتت هذه الدعوى بهذا المنهج، ولكن بعد لم يظهر لنا إلا وجود السبب، فأما كونه قديماً أو حادثاً، وصفاتُه.. فلم يظهر بعد، فلنشتغل، والله الموفق هو الهادي (۱) من عدم التعرض لثبوت دعوانا، بل ندعي استحالة دعوى الخصم، وهذا يكفينا مؤنة إثبات دعوانا . انظر ( ص ۸۱ ) .
Difficulty: We grant that it has (1) and it is less than the rotations of the sun; for it is known necessarily that one-third of a thing is less than one-tenth of the thing. And the moon rotates twelve times in a year, so the number of rotations of the sun is, for example, half a sixth of the rotations of the moon, and each one has no end, and some of them are less than some, and that is manifestly impossible. For if it is said: The things God is capable of, according to you, have no end, and likewise His objects of knowledge have no difference, and the objects of knowledge are more than the objects of power; for the Essence of the Eternal and His attributes are known to Him, and likewise the two infinites, so how are they different? The infinite of continuous existence is not an object of power? (2) Is power with infinity of knowledge? The difficulty. We say: When we say there is no end to His objects of power, we do not mean by it what we mean by our saying there is no end to His objects of knowledge. Rather we mean by it that God has an attribute expressed by power, by which origination is achieved (3), and this achievability never ceases (4). And under our saying 'this achievability does not cease' there is no affirmation of things, much less that meanings from the source of error for this are described as finite or infinite. Rather this error occurs to one who looks at words and sees the balance of the words 'objects of knowledge' and 'objects of power' in terms of derivation in language, so he thinks that the intended meaning is the same; far from it! There is no correspondence between them at all. Then under our saying 'the objects of knowledge have no end' there is also a secret that contradicts what first comes to mind; for what first comes to mind is the affirmation of things called infinite objects of knowledge, and that is impossible. Rather the things are existents, and they are finite, but explaining that requires lengthening. (1) According to what the proponent of the eternity of the world holds. (2) So it would follow from that a difference between the two infinites? And their objection can be formulated: His knowledge pertains to the necessary, the contingent, and the impossible, unlike power which pertains only to the contingent. (3) And he did not say 'origination and annihilation are achieved by it' because of disagreement about power's relation to annihilating a possible after its existence. (4) So we expressed the impossibility of the cessation of this achievability by the infinity of power. And the objection has been repelled by uncovering the meaning of negating end from the objects of power. So examining the second side—namely the objects of knowledge—is unnecessary for repelling the obligation. For the correctness of this principle has become clear by the third method among the methods of proofs mentioned in the fourth preface of the book. And with this the existence of the Maker is known; for the syllogism we mentioned has become clear, which is our saying: The world is originated, and every originated thing has a cause; so the world has a cause. So this claim has been established by this method, but only the existence of the cause has become apparent to us; as for its being eternal or originated, and its attributes, they have not yet become apparent. So let us occupy ourselves [with that], and God is the grantor of success and the guide. (1) [This is] from not engaging in proving our claim; rather we claim the impossibility of the opponent's claim, and this suffices us the burden of proving our claim. See p. 81.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage contains an objection against the infinity of divine attributes and Ghazali's response, concluding with the proof of the existence of the Creator. Ghazali first presents a difficulty: if the opponent grants that the world is originated, then the number of solar rotations is less than lunar rotations, yet both are infinite, which is impossible. This sets up the objection: opponents may say that God's objects of power are infinite, and His objects of knowledge are also infinite, but objects of knowledge are more (since they include His essence and attributes, which are not objects of power). So how can two infinites differ in magnitude? The objection attempts to create a paradox regarding the divine attributes. Ghazali's answer clarifies that when Muslims say God's objects of power are infinite, they do not mean the same kind of infinity as for knowledge. Rather, 'no end to objects of power' means that the attribute of power is such that the ability to create never ceases; it is not an affirmation of an infinite set of objects. The infinity of knowledge, on the other hand, is understood differently: it is not an infinite set of things, because actual things are finite. The misunderstanding arises from looking only at linguistic forms. Once this is clarified, the objection is repelled. Ghazali then concludes that this third method (referring to the third proof from the preface) establishes that the world is originated and needs a cause, thus proving the existence of a Maker. However, further investigation is needed to determine the nature and attributes of that cause. The footnote clarifies that this argument proceeds by disproving the opponent's claim rather than directly proving one's own.

العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the infinity of divine power and knowledge are of the same kind and imply an actual infinite set of objects, leading to a contradiction.
Correction: Ghazali explains that the infinity of power refers to never-ending ability to create, while the infinity of knowledge is about the comprehensiveness of divine knowledge, not a quantity of objects. The two are not comparable in the same sense.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the main reason for the error in the objection about infinite divine power and knowledge?
A) The opponent denies the existence of the Creator.
B) The opponent confuses the linguistic form of 'objects of power' and 'objects of knowledge' while the meanings are different.
C) The opponent claims that power and knowledge are the same attribute.
D) The opponent does not accept that the world is originated.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali resolves the paradox of infinite divine attributes by clarifying that 'no end' in power means continual ability, while 'no end' in knowledge refers to the comprehensiveness of divine knowledge, not an infinite set of objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own understanding of God, have you unconsciously assumed that His attributes function like human abilities—limited and competing with each other? How does Ghazali's correction of the paradox challenge you to purify your mental image of God from such hidden anthropomorphic assumptions? What specific step will you take this week to worship God as He truly is, not as your mind imagines Him?
?
You just learned that 'infinity' for divine power means a never-ceasing ability to act. Consider your prayer life: do you truly trust that God's power to respond is always present, or do you sometimes feel He is 'out of power' or 'too busy' with other matters? How can you consciously affirm the continuous nature of God's power in your next supplication?
?
Ghazali distinguishes between knowing about God (comprehensiveness of knowledge) and the actual living out of that knowledge. Is your faith merely a collection of correct beliefs, or does it translate into a life that constantly relies on God's power? What is one area of your life where you have not yet allowed God's power to intervene because you were satisfied with just knowing the correct doctrine?

Refutation of Annihilation by Power
Ghazali refutes the claim that God annihilates by power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that annihilation by power is not a genuine act because it results in nothing.

Core Idea
Annihilation is not an act because non-existence is not a thing.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a subtle theological claim: that God annihilates substances by His power. He unpacks the logic of 'act' and 'power' to show that annihilation cannot be an act of power because it produces nothing. This argument reinforces the principle that God's power is exercised only in bringing about existence, not in negating it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then the translation. Identify the main claim being refuted.
Notice the tight logical progression.
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify the premise: act by power is act of essence. Then: annihilation would be negation of existence. Then: negation of existence is nothing. Therefore: no act occurred.
3
Consider the implication
If no act occurred, then power was not used, and God remains unchanged. What does this say about God's power?
Only existence is a product of power.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Why might someone think annihilation is an act? How does Ghazali's argument correct this?
Change to non-existence is not a real act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة (١) ، فلا يتصوَّرُ أن يقول : الفعلُ الواقع بالقدرة فعل ذلك الذات ؛ فإنها أزلية ، وإنّما فعله نفي وجود الذات ، ونفي وجود الذات ليس شيئاً . . فإذن ما فعل شيئاً
وإذا صدق قولنا : ما فعل شيئاً .. صدق قولنا : إنه لم يستعمل القدرة في أمر ألبتة ، فبقي كما كان ، ولم يفعل شيئا (٢)
Power (1): So it is not conceivable that one says: the act occurring by power is an act of that essence; for it is eternal, but its act is the negation of the existence of the essence, and the negation of the existence of the essence is nothing. So then it did not do anything. And if our statement 'it did not do anything' is true, then our statement 'it did not use power in a matter at all' is true, so it remained as it was and did not do anything (2).
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines a specific argument against the notion that God annihilates things by His power. Ghazali begins with the premise that if an act occurs by power, it is an act of the agent. He then considers the case where someone claims that God annihilates a substance by His power. Ghazali argues that such an 'act' would be the negation of the existence of the substance, but negation of existence is not a real thing (laysa shay'an). Therefore, no act has occurred. Since no act occurred, the agent (God) has not used His power at all; He remains as He was, having done nothing. The argument relies on the principle that an act must produce something existent; annihilation produces nothing, so it is not a genuine act of power.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra, power)
إعدام (i'dam, annihilation)
نفي وجود (nafy al-wujud, negation of existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Annihilation is an act because it causes change.
Correction: Ghazali argues that change to non-existence is not a real act because non-existence is not a thing.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it not correct to say that God annihilates something by His power?
A) Because God's power is only creative, not destructive.
B) Because annihilation is the negation of existence, which is nothing, so no act occurs.
C) Because annihilation would require a different kind of power.
D) Because God never causes non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Annihilation cannot be an act of power because non-existence is not a thing, so no act has occurred.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that non-existence is nothing and cannot be an act of divine power. Does this change how you view your own existence? Are you aware that your continued existence is a real act of God's power every moment? How will you remind yourself of this today?
?
If the destruction of something is not an act of power, then what does that say about the objects you cling to? Will you let go of the illusion that anything can be annihilated by your worry? How does this lesson make you trust God's preservation?
?
Think of a loss you have experienced. You might have felt that God 'took away' something from you. But according to this argument, non-existence is not something God does. Can you honestly accept that the loss was not an act? What does that imply about your relationship with God and your attachment to worldly things?

The Non-Existence of Atoms by Cessation of Motion and Rest
Ghazali explains the Ash'ari view on the annihilation of atoms via withdrawal of sustaining accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theology explains the annihilation of atoms through the removal of the condition of their existence (motion and rest).

Core Idea
Atoms cease to exist not by direct destruction, but when God withholds the accidents that sustain them.

In Ash'ari atomism, the continued existence of atoms is contingent upon God's ongoing creation of accidents. Ghazali clarifies a subtle but crucial point: atoms are not annihilated by a separate act of destruction; rather, they simply vanish when the condition for their existence—the creation of motion and rest—is no longer present. This passage illuminates the Ash'ari view of divine causation and the contingency of the material world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording settle in your mind.
Note the use of the term 'jawāhir' (atoms).
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that atoms cease to exist when motion and rest are not created in them. This means their existence is conditional.
The condition (sharṭ) is the creation of these accidents.
3
Understand the analogy
Ghazali compares the atom to a wick and the creation of accidents to oil. Just as a wick goes out when oil is cut off, the atom perishes when the 'supply' of motion/rest is withheld.
This illustrates that atoms do not have independent existence.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This doctrine emphasizes that God's power is exercised through continuous creation, not just initial creation. Atoms depend on God at every moment.
This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواهر : فانعدامها بألا تخلق فيها الحركة والسكون (١) ، فينقطع شرط وجودها ، ولا يعقل بقاؤها (١) أي : يقطع عنها المدد، فتنعدم بنفسها؛ كالفتيلة إذا انقطع عنها الزيت.. انطفات بنفسها، هذا ما ذهب إليه الإمام الأشعري رحمه الله تعالى . انظر : حاشية البيجوري على جوهرة التوحيد : ( ص ١٢١ )
As for atoms (jawāhir): their non-existence is by not creating motion and rest in them (1), so the condition of their existence is cut off, and their remaining is not conceivable. (1) That is: the supply is cut off from them, so they become non-existent by themselves; like the wick when the oil is cut off from it.. it goes out by itself. This is what Imam al-Ashʿarī, may Allah have mercy on him, held. See: Ḥāshiyat al-Bayjūrī ʿalā Jawharat al-Tawḥīd (p. 121).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the mode of non-existence for atoms (jawāhir) in the Ashʿarī framework. The author states that atoms cease to exist when motion and rest are not created in them. The creation of motion and rest is the condition (sharṭ) for their continued existence; when that condition is cut off, the atom cannot remain. The footnote clarifies by analogy: just as a lamp wick goes out when the oil supply is cut off, the atom perishes by itself when the divine creative act (the 'supply') is withheld. This is attributed to Imam al-Ashʿarī. The argument preserves the Ashʿarī position that atoms are not annihilated by a separate act of destruction but rather depend continuously on God's creation of accidents for their existence.

Key Terms
جواهر
حركة
سكون
شرط الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how do atoms (jawāhir) cease to exist?
A) By a direct act of annihilation by God
B) By not creating motion and rest in them, cutting off the condition of their existence
C) By their own choice
D) By being transformed into another substance

Key Takeaway
Atoms do not possess inherent existence; they depend on God's continuous creation of accidents, and when that creation ceases, they simply cease to be.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence in every moment depends on Allah's continuous creation of conditions (like motion and rest for atoms), do you truly live with the awareness that you are sustained by His will each second? What would change in your daily habits if you fully internalized this dependence?
?
The wick does not light itself; it needs the oil. In what areas of your life do you act as though you have 'self-sustaining fuel'—your own intellect, strength, or resources—rather than recognizing that even your ability to move and think is given to you fresh each instant? How can you train yourself to feel that need for Allah's constant support?
?
When you sin, do you realize that you are misusing the very accidents (motion, life, health) that Allah continuously creates to sustain you? How would this understanding affect your mindfulness of Allah (taqwa) before you act, knowing that He could withdraw the condition of your existence at any moment?

Two Approaches to Hadiths of Attributes
Nawawi enumerates two madhhabs on attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two mainstream Sunni approaches to hadiths of divine attributes: tafwid and ta'wil.

Core Idea
Two valid ways: believe without interpreting, or interpret befitting Allah.

Imam Nawawi explains that hadiths about Allah's attributes are handled by either affirming them without explaining how while transcendent, or by interpreting them metaphorically. This lesson unpacks these two approaches and shows how they both avoid anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read Nawawi's statement
Read the Arabic and translation of Nawawi's words on the two madhhabs. Notice his careful phrasing.
Nawawi mentions these approaches elsewhere in his works.
2
Identify the two approaches
Find the descriptions of tafwid and ta'wil in the text. Tafwid: believe without delving into meaning. Ta'wil: interpret in a manner befitting Allah.
Both affirm tanzih (transcendence).
3
Analyze the example of ta'wil
Nawawi provides a ta'wil for the slave girl hadith: the test was about her tawhid, not Allah's location. See how it preserves divine transcendence.
The example shows how ta'wil works in practice without denying the text.
4
Reflect on the consensus
Consider that both approaches are accepted in Sunni Islam; neither implies anthropomorphism.
This is a key point in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَالَ الإِمَامُ النَّوَوِيُّ فِي حَدِيثِ الْجَارِيَةِ هَذَا: (هَذَا الْحَدِيثُ مِنْ أَحَادِيثِ الصِّفَاتِ، وَفِيهَا مَذْهَبَانِ تَقَدَّمَ ذِكْرُهُمَا مَرَّاتٍ فِي كِتَابِ الإِيمَانِ؛ أَحَدُهُمَا: الإِيمَانُ بِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ خَوْضٍ فِي مَعْنَاهُ مَعَ اعْتِقَادِ أَنَّ اللهَ تَعَالَى لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَتَنْزِيهُهُ عَنْ سِمَاتِ الْمَخْلُوقَاتِ، وَالثَّانِي: تَأْوِيلُهُ بِمَا يَلِيقُ بِهِ، فَمَنْ قَالَ بِهَذَا.. قَالَ: كَانَ الْمُرَادُ امْتِحَانُهَا: هَلْ هِيَ مُوَحِّدَةٌ تُقِرُّ بِأَنَّ الْخَالِقَ الْمُدِيرَ الْفَعَّالَ هُوَ اللهُ وَحْدَهُ، وَهُوَ الَّذِي إِذَا دَعَاهُ الدَّاعِي.. اسْتَقْبَلَ السَّمَاءَ كَمَا إِذَا صَلَّى الْمُصَلِّي.. اسْتَقْبَلَ الْكَعْبَةَ، وَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ لأَنَّهُ مُنْحَصِرٌ فِي السَّمَاءِ، كَمَا أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مُنْحَصِراً فِي جِهَةِ الْكَعْبَةِ). (شَرْحُ صَحِيحِ مُسْلِمٍ، ٢٤/٥). قَالَ الْقَاضِي عِيَاضٌ: (لَا خِلَافَ بَيْنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ قَاطِبَةً فَقِيهُهُمْ وَمُحَدِّثُهُمْ وَمُتَكَلِّمُهُمْ وَنَظَّارُهُمْ وَمُقَلِّدُهُمْ أَنَّ الظَّوَاهِرَ الْوَارِدَةَ بِذِكْرِ اللهِ تَعَالَى فِي السَّمَاءِ؛ كَقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: أَمِنْتُمْ مَنْ فِي السَّمَاءِ وَنَحْوِهِ.. لَيْسَتْ عَلَى ظَاهِرِهَا، بَلْ مُتَأَوَّلَةٌ عِنْدَ جَمِيعِهِمْ).
Imam Nawawi said regarding this hadith of the slave girl: 'This hadith is among the hadiths of the attributes, and there are two schools concerning them, which have been mentioned many times in the Book of Faith: one of them is to believe in them without delving into their meaning, while holding the belief that Allah, may He be exalted, is not like anything, and transcending Him from the characteristics of created beings; and the second is to interpret them in a manner befitting Him. Whoever adopts this says: The intent was to test her: whether she is a monotheist who affirms that the Creator, the Manager, the Doer is Allah alone, and that when a supplicant supplicates, he faces the sky just as when a worshipper prays, he faces the Kaaba, and this is not because He is confined to the sky, just as He is not confined to the direction of the Kaaba.' (Sharh Sahih Muslim, 24/5). Qadi Iyad said: 'There is no disagreement among all Muslims, their jurist, their hadith scholar, their theologian, their rationalist, and their layman, that the apparent meanings of the verses that mention Allah in the sky, such as His saying: "Do you feel secure from Him who is in the sky" and the like, are not upon their literal meanings, but are interpreted metaphorically by all of them.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage explains the two mainstream methodological approaches to hadiths of the attributes (ahadith al-sifat). Imam Nawawi states that the first approach is to affirm the text without delving into its meaning (tafwid) while maintaining absolute transcendence (tanzih). The second approach is to interpret (ta'wil) the text in a way befitting Allah's majesty. Nawawi gives an example of ta'wil for the slave girl hadith: the test was about her tawhid, not about Allah's spatial location. Then Qadi Iyad asserts a strong consensus (ijma') among all categories of Muslims—jurists, hadith scholars, theologians, rationalists, and laymen—that verses like 'Who is in the sky' are not literal, but are interpreted metaphorically (mutawwalat). This consensus is a powerful refutation of those who insist on a literal interpretation that implies direction.

Key Terms
صفات
تفويض
تأويل
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only valid approach is a literal reading of hadiths of attributes.
Correction: Nawawi shows two valid approaches: tafwid and ta'wil, both rejecting literal anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nawawi, what are the two madhhabs regarding hadiths of attributes?
A) Literal interpretation and allegorical interpretation.
B) Tafwid (believing without interpretation) and ta'wil (interpretation befitting Allah).
C) Denial and affirmation.
D) Anthropomorphism and negation.

Key Takeaway
Sunni theology accepts two valid ways to handle hadiths of attributes: tafwid and ta'wil, both avoiding anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith that describes Allah's hand or face, what is your immediate reaction? Do you instinctively imagine something? How does knowing the two madhhabs change the way you should respond? This lesson demands you choose a path—either trust without interpretation, or interpret with proper tools. Which path have you been avoiding, and why?
?
The slave girl's simple answer 'In the sky' satisfied the Prophet. Yet Nawawi interprets it as a test of monotheism. Are you willing to accept that your understanding of a text might need to be refined by scholars? Or do you cling to a literal reading to avoid discomfort? Consider a specific attribute hadith you find difficult. Can you honestly say you've approached it with either tafwid or ta'wil? What would it cost you to admit you don't know the exact meaning?

Consensus on Non-Literal Interpretation of Spatial Verses
Qadi Iyad asserts consensus on non-literal tafsir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unanimous consensus of Muslims that verses mentioning Allah in the sky are not literal.

Core Idea
All Muslims agree: spatial references to Allah are metaphorical.

Qadi Iyad claims there is no disagreement among any category of Muslims that verses describing Allah as being in the sky are interpreted metaphorically, not literally. This lesson explores the strength of this consensus and its implication for theology.

Guided Path
1
Read Qadi Iyad's statement
Read the Arabic and translation of his claim of consensus. Notice the listing of various groups.
He includes jurists, hadith scholars, theologians, rationalists, and laymen.
2
Understand the consensus claim
Note that he says 'no disagreement among all Muslims' regarding non-literal interpretation.
This is a strong claim of unanimity.
3
Identify the verse referenced
The example given is from the Quran: 'Do you feel secure from Him who is in the sky'.
This verse is often cited by those who affirm direction, but consensus says it's metaphorical.
4
Connect to the two approaches
This consensus supports both tafwid and ta'wil, as long as they reject literal interpretation.
So both approaches agree on rejecting literal meaning.

وَقَالَ الإِمَامُ النَّوَوِيُّ فِي حَدِيثِ الْجَارِيَةِ هَذَا: (هَذَا الْحَدِيثُ مِنْ أَحَادِيثِ الصِّفَاتِ، وَفِيهَا مَذْهَبَانِ تَقَدَّمَ ذِكْرُهُمَا مَرَّاتٍ فِي كِتَابِ الإِيمَانِ؛ أَحَدُهُمَا: الإِيمَانُ بِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ خَوْضٍ فِي مَعْنَاهُ مَعَ اعْتِقَادِ أَنَّ اللهَ تَعَالَى لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَتَنْزِيهُهُ عَنْ سِمَاتِ الْمَخْلُوقَاتِ، وَالثَّانِي: تَأْوِيلُهُ بِمَا يَلِيقُ بِهِ، فَمَنْ قَالَ بِهَذَا.. قَالَ: كَانَ الْمُرَادُ امْتِحَانُهَا: هَلْ هِيَ مُوَحِّدَةٌ تُقِرُّ بِأَنَّ الْخَالِقَ الْمُدِيرَ الْفَعَّالَ هُوَ اللهُ وَحْدَهُ، وَهُوَ الَّذِي إِذَا دَعَاهُ الدَّاعِي.. اسْتَقْبَلَ السَّمَاءَ كَمَا إِذَا صَلَّى الْمُصَلِّي.. اسْتَقْبَلَ الْكَعْبَةَ، وَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ لأَنَّهُ مُنْحَصِرٌ فِي السَّمَاءِ، كَمَا أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مُنْحَصِراً فِي جِهَةِ الْكَعْبَةِ). The Metaphor of Finger and Arm for Divine Power
Ghazali explains the metaphor of finger and arm as signifying divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali uses the metaphor of finger and arm to explain divine power while affirming God's transcendence.

Core Idea
God's attributes are not literal limbs; they are metaphors for His power and knowledge.

This lesson explores a crucial method in Islamic theology: interpreting anthropomorphic expressions metaphorically. Ghazali contrasts the ignorant and knowledgeable understanding of such terms, showing that the true meaning points to divine attributes. The passage examines the metaphor of a finger as a symbol of power, and the Qur'anic term 'ma'iyya' (accompaniment) as a symbol of knowledge and encompassment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between the ignorant and knowledgeable person's understanding.
Pay attention to the shift from literal to metaphorical interpretation.
2
Identify the two metaphors
The passage presents two examples: the finger/arm metaphor and the 'ma'iyya' (accompaniment) metaphor. Identify what each metaphor signifies for the knowledgeable person.
The finger metaphor points to power; 'ma'iyya' points to knowledge and encompassment.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Why does Ghazali use such metaphors? What principle is he defending? The commentary will help you connect this to God's transcendence.
This lesson establishes that anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran must be understood metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذراعاً فإنه عند الجاهل يخيلُ عضوين مركَّبين من اللحم والعظم والعصب مشتملين على الأنامل والأظفار ، نابتين من الكف ، وعند العالم يدلُّ على المعنى المستعار له دون الموضوع له ، وهو ما كان الإصبع له ، وكأنه سمى الإصبع قدرته ؛ لأنَّ سرّ الإصبع وروحه وحقيقته هو القدرة على التقليب كيف يشاء ، كما دلت المعيَّةُ في قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ مَعَكُمْ أَيْنَ مَا كنتُم ﴾ على ما تراد المعية له ؛ وهو العلم والإحاطة
A forearm span: for the ignorant person, it suggests two limbs composed of flesh, bone, and nerve, comprising fingers and nails, sprouting from the palm. But for the knowledgeable person, it indicates the metaphorical meaning, not the literal one, which is that which has the finger, and as if He called the finger His power; because the secret of the finger, its spirit, and its reality is the ability to turn however He wills. Similarly, the term 'ma'iyya' (accompaniment) in His statement, 'And He is with you wherever you are,' indicates what is intended by accompaniment: knowledge and encompassment.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the metaphorical interpretation of bodily parts attributed to God. The author contrasts the ignorant understanding (literal limbs) with the knowledgeable understanding (metaphorical meaning). The finger symbolizes power, as its essence is the ability to manipulate. Likewise, God's 'ma'iyya' (being with creation) is not spatial but denotes knowledge and encompassment.

Metaphorical Interpretation of the Hadith of Drawing Near
Ghazali explains the metaphor of drawing near and trotting in hadith qudsi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith of drawing near must be interpreted metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'drawing near' is a metaphor for His abundant mercy, not physical movement.

Imam Ghazali now turns to a well-known hadith qudsi that might suggest God physically moves towards the believer. He explains that the ignorant take it literally, while the rational person recognizes it as a metaphor for God's outpouring of mercy. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic hadith and its literal translation carefully to see the words that might be misunderstood.
Notice the use of 'shibr' and 'dhiraa' (span and cubit).
2
Contrast the two interpretations
Identify how the ignorant understanding differs from the rational one as explained by Ghazali.
The ignorant take 'trotting' literally as running; the rational see it as a metaphor.
3
Grasp the intended meaning
Understand that the true meaning is about God's mercy outpouring more intensely than the servant's obedience.
This preserves God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه : من تقرب إلي وكقوله تعالى في الحديث القدسي : ( مَنْ تقرَّبَ إليَّ شبراً تقرَّبْتُ إليه ذراعاً ، ومَنْ أتاني يمشي أتيته هرولة ) فإن الهرولة عند الجاهل تدل على نقل الأقدام وشدَّة العدو ، وكذا الإتيان يدل على القرب في وعند العاقل يدل على المعنى المطلوب من قرب المسافة بين الناس ، وهو قرب الكرامة والإنعام ، وأن معناه : أنَّ رحمتي ونعمتي أشدُّ انصباباً إلى عبادي من طاعتهم إليَّ
And from that: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a span, I draw near to him by a cubit; and whoever comes to Me walking, I come to him at a trot.' For trotting in the view of the ignorant indicates moving the feet and intense running, and likewise 'coming' indicates proximity. But in the view of the rational person, it indicates the intended meaning of proximity of distance among people, which is closeness in honor and bounty. Its meaning is that My mercy and bounty pour more intensely upon My servants than their obedience to Me.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the hadith qudsi about drawing near to God. The author distinguishes between the ignorant understanding of harwala (trotting) as physical movement and the rational understanding as a metaphor for God's abundant mercy and kindness. The real meaning is that God's grace is more intense towards the servant than the servant's effort towards God.

The Metaphor of Longing (Shawq) for God
Ghazali explains the metaphor of longing in a hadith qudsi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine longing must be interpreted metaphorically, not literally, and how it parallels other divine attributes like anger and pleasure.

Core Idea
Divine longing is not emotion but a metaphor for God's bestowal of mercy.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali addresses a hadith that speaks of God's longing for His righteous servants. Since literal longing implies need and deficiency, which are impossible for God, he explains it as a metaphor (isti'-āra) for the cause that leads to blessings. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how it parallels other scriptural metaphors like God's anger and pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and identify the key term
Start by reading the hadith quote, focusing on the term 'shawq' (longing). Notice that it attributes a human-like emotion to God.
Literal reading leads to theological problems.
2
Understand why literal meaning is impossible
Ghazali points out that literal shawq involves pain and need, which are deficiencies. Since God is perfect and free from need, the literal meaning cannot apply.
This is a key principle of divine transcendence (tanzih).
3
See the metaphorical explanation
Ghazali explains that shawq is used metaphorically for the cause (sabab) of God's acceptance and outpouring of blessings. He then draws a parallel with anger and pleasure, which are metaphors for God's will to reward or punish.
This shows a consistent method for interpreting anthropomorphic expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه: «الأشد شوقاً طال شوقُ الأبرار إلى لقائي ، وأنا إلى لقائِهِمْ لأشدُّ شوقاً» تعالى عمّا يفهم من معنى لفظ الشوق بالوضع ؛ فإنه نوع ألم ، وحاجة إلى استراحة ، وهو عين النقص ، ولكنَّ الشوق سبب لقبول المشتاق إليه ، والإقبال عليه وإفاضة النعم لديه ، فعبَّر به عن المسبب كما عبر بالغضب والرضا عن إرادة الثواب والعقاب اللذين هما ثمرة الغضب والرضا ومسبّباه في العادة
And from that: 'The greater in longing: the longing of the righteous for meeting Me is intense, and I am more intense in longing for meeting them.' He is exalted above what is understood from the literal meaning of the word 'shawq'; for it is a type of pain and a need for relief, and that is pure deficiency. But longing is a cause for acceptance of the longed-for one, turning towards him, and pouring down blessings upon him. So it is used metaphorically for the effect, just as anger and pleasure are used metaphorically for the will to reward and punish, which are the fruit of anger and pleasure and their customary effects.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a hadith about mutual longing. The author clarifies that the literal meaning of longing (shawq) as emotional pain cannot apply to God. Instead, it is a metaphor (isti’ara) for the cause of receiving blessings and mercy. Similarly, anger and pleasure are used metaphorically for God's will to reward or punish.

Key Terms
شوق
استعارة
غضب
رضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taking 'longing' literally means God feels pain and need.
Correction: Longing is a metaphor for the causal relationship between God's mercy and the believer's state.

Checkpoint

A) As a literal emotion
B) As a metaphor for the cause of bestowing mercy
C) As proof of God's need
D) As a physical condition

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of longing, anger, and pleasure are metaphors for His actions, not literal emotions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear that God longs for you, does your heart tremble? Now that you know it is a metaphor for His bestowal of blessings, does that change how you seek His nearness? Will you pursue actions that draw His acceptance, knowing that His 'longing' is real in its effect?
?
Do you sometimes imagine God's anger as human rage, feeling distant or afraid? How does understanding it as a metaphor for His will to discipline you transform your view of repentance and hope?
?
If God's longing is not a feeling but a cause for His blessings, does your longing for Him become purer, free from expecting emotional reciprocity? What must you do today to turn your heart fully toward the One who turns toward you?

The Metaphor of the Black Stone as God's Right Hand
Ghazali refutes literal interpretation and explains metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith about the black stone being God's right hand must be interpreted metaphorically, not literally.

Core Idea
God's right hand is a metaphor, not a physical limb.

Ghazali tackles a famous hadith that could be misunderstood literally. He uses reason and language to show that the black stone is called God's right hand as a metaphor for the handshake of allegiance. This lesson explains his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the literal interpretation
Start by reading the hadith and the ignorant person's literal understanding. Notice the assumption that 'right hand' refers to a physical limb.
2
Follow Ghazali's reasoning against literalism
See how Ghazali uses logic: if God is on the Throne, His right hand cannot be in the Ka'ba, and it cannot be a stone. This shows the literal reading is impossible.
3
Understand the metaphorical meaning
Recognize that 'right hand' is borrowed (isti'ara) for the handshake (musafaha) because pilgrims touch and kiss the stone as they would a king's hand.
The intelligent person grasps this intuitively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لما قال : ( الحجر الأسود يمينُ الله في أرضه ) .. يظنُّ الجاهلُ أنَّه أراد به اليمين المقابل للشمال ، الذي هو عضو مركب من لحم ودم وعظم ، منقسم بخمسة أصابع ، ثم إن فتح بصيرته . . علم أنه : إن كان على العرش .. فلا يكون يمينه في الكعبة ، ثم لا يكون حجراً أسود فيدرك بأدنى مُسْكَة أنه استعير للمصافحة ؛ فإنه أمر باستلام الحجر وتقبيله كما يؤمر بتقبيل يمين الملوك ، فاستعير اللفظ لذلك والكامل العقل البصير باللغة لا تعظم عنده هذه الأمور ، بل يفهم معانيها على البديهة
And likewise when he said: 'The black stone is the right hand of God on His earth.' The ignorant person thinks that He intended the right hand opposite the left, which is a limb composed of flesh, blood, and bone, divided into five fingers. Then if he opens his insight, he knows that if He is on the Throne, His right hand cannot be in the Ka'ba; then further, it cannot be a black stone. So he understands with the slightest reasoning that it is borrowed for the handshake; because He commanded touching and kissing the stone just as one is commanded to kiss the hands of kings. Thus the expression is borrowed for that. And the person of perfect intellect, perceptive of language, is not awestruck by these matters; rather, he understands their meanings instinctively.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the hadith 'The black stone is the right hand of God on earth.' The author argues that taking it literally would be impossible because God is on the Throne, not located in the Ka'ba, and the stone is not a physical limb. Instead, it is a metaphor (isti’ara) for the act of shaking hands, because pilgrims touch and kiss the stone just as they would kiss a king's hand. The intelligent person grasps this intuitively.

Key Terms
يمين
حجر أسود
استعارة
مصافحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think God has a literal right hand on earth, meaning a physical limb located in the Ka'ba.
Correction: Ghazali shows this is impossible because God is on the Throne and the stone is not a limb; the phrase is a metaphor for the handshake of allegiance.

Checkpoint
What is the metaphorical meaning of the black stone being called the right hand of God?
A) It is a literal limb
B) It symbolizes God's power
C) It is a metaphor for the handshake of allegiance
D) It means God is present in the stone

Key Takeaway
The hadith about the black stone must be understood metaphorically as a symbol of allegiance, not literally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Ghazali says the person of perfect intellect is not awestruck by literal anthropomorphic expressions but understands their meanings instinctively. Are you sometimes awestruck or confused by verses or hadith that describe Allah in human-like terms? What literal-minded assumptions about Allah do you hold that you need to repent from? Will you commit to seeking the metaphorical interpretation that befits Allah's majesty?
?

Are you prepared to defend Allah's transcendence even if it means challenging common folk beliefs? Analyzing the Possible Meanings of Istawa (Established)
Ghazali enumerates possible meanings of istawa and identifies the intended one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali systematically enumerates possible relationships (nisba) implied by the term 'istawa' and identifies the rationally and linguistically acceptable meaning, thereby preserving divine transcendence while accepting scriptural language.

Core Idea
Istawa is a relationship, not a physical position; Ghazali shows which relationship is intended.

When the Qur'an says Allah 'established Himself over the Throne' (istawa), what does that mean? Ghazali approaches this by first admitting that istawa implies a relationship between Allah and the Throne. But relationships can be of many kinds. In this passage, he lists all possible categories of relationships that could be meant, then eliminates those that are rationally impossible or linguistically unsuitable. The surviving category is the intended meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting point
Read the passage and note that Ghazali asserts istawa is a relation (nisba) of the Throne to Allah. He is not denying the term, but analyzing its implications.
This is a middle-ground approach between anthropomorphism and outright denial.
2
Identify the five possible relations
Look for the five types: known, willed, powerful over, locus (like an accident), place (like a body). List them and think about what each would imply about Allah and the Throne.
Some are rationally impossible for Allah, like being a locus or place.
3
Determine the elimination criteria
Ghazali says some are rationally impossible (like spatial location) and some are not suitable as metaphors. Ask: which ones are impossible? Which are unsuitable?
He leaves the conclusion open: the correct one is the one that remains.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Notice that Ghazali uses rational and linguistic criteria to select the intended meaning. This is a key method in kalam to interpret ambiguous verses without falling into anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلنرجع إلى معنى الاستواء والنزول : أما الاستواء : فهو نسبة العرش إليه لا محالة ، ولا يمكن أن يكون مناقشة معاني الاستواء والخلوص للعرش إليه نسبة إلا بكونه : معلوماً ، أو مراداً ، أو مقدوراً عليه ، أو محلاً مثل محل العرض ، أو مكاناً مثل مستقر الجسم . ولكن بعض هذه النسب تستحيل عقلاً ، وبعضها لا يصلح للاستعارة له ، فإن كان في جملة هذه النسب - مع أنه لا نسبة سواها - نسبة لا يحيلها العقل ، ولا ينبو عنها اللفظ . . فليعلم أنها المراد
Let us return to the meaning of istawa (establishing) and nuzul (descending). As for istawa: it is a relation (nisba) of the Throne to Him without doubt. It is not possible to discuss the meanings of istawa and pinpoint the relation of the Throne to Him except by it being: known, or willed, or powerful over it, or a locus like the locus of an accident, or a place like the location of a body. But some of these relations are impossible rationally, and some are not suitable for metaphorical use for Him. If among these relations - and there is no relation besides them - there is a relation that reason does not deem impossible and language does not reject, then let it be known that it is the intended meaning.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the term 'istawa' (established) when applied to God and the Throne. The author asserts that istawa expresses a relationship (nisba) of the Throne to God. He then enumerates five possible types of relationship: (1) known (ma'lum), (2) willed (murad), (3) powerful over it (maqdur 'alayh), (4) being a locus like an accident (mahall mithl mahall al-'arad), (5) being a place like a body's position (makan mithl mustaqarr al-jism). Some are impossible rationally (e.g., spatial location), some are not appropriate metaphors. The correct meaning is that which is neither rationally impossible nor linguistically incongruous.

Key Terms
استواء
نسبة
عرش
محل
مكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think istawa implies physical sitting or location on the Throne.
Correction: Ghazali shows that istawa is a relation among a finite set of possible relations; the correct one is neither spatial nor bodily, but rationally and linguistically sound.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct way to understand 'istawa' (establishing) in relation to the Throne?
A) As a physical sitting
B) As a relation among a finite set that is rationally possible and linguistically proper
C) As the Throne being a location for God
D) As a metaphor for movement

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that scriptural terms like istawa must be interpreted through rational and linguistic analysis to avoid anthropomorphism while affirming the divine attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the verse 'ar-Rahmanu 'ala al-arshi istawa', do you subconsciously form an image of Allah in your mind? What does this passage teach you about the danger of such images, and are you willing to actively replace them with a relation that remains undefined by human spatial concepts?
?
Ghazali insists that any valid interpretation of a divine attribute must pass both rational scrutiny and linguistic appropriateness. Have you ever accepted a simplistic interpretation of a divine attribute without engaging your intellect? What would it cost you to commit to a more disciplined approach when reading Allah's names and attributes?
?
If the intended meaning of 'istawa' is a real relation between Allah and the Throne that reason and language affirm, but its exact nature remains unknown to you, does that unsettle you? Can you worship a Lord whose actions toward His creation are real yet beyond full conceptual grasp, or do you need to know exactly how He relates to His creation? Answer honestly.

Analyzing the Meaning of Istiwa (Establishing over the Throne)
Ghazali analyzes possible meanings of 'istawa' and concludes it means dominance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses rational and linguistic analysis to determine the correct interpretation of 'istawa' without anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Ghazali shows that 'istawa' means dominance, not physical position.

How do we understand verses about God 'establishing over the Throne' without imagining a body? Imam al-Ghazali applies a systematic method: he identifies all possible meanings of a term and tests each against reason and language. In this passage, he does exactly that for 'istawa', concluding that only one meaning fits both criteria.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for its overall flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Ghazali sets up a systematic analysis.
The passage is dense but logically structured.
2
Identify the five possible relations
Ghazali lists five ways the Throne could relate to God: being known, being willed, being under power, being a locus, or being a place. Find each in the text.
These are the only logical possibilities he considers.
3
Note the elimination criteria
He eliminates 'locus' and 'place' as rationally impossible (they imply corporeality). He eliminates 'known' and 'willed' as linguistically unsuitable (the word 'istawa' does not convey them).
Only 'under power' remains.
4
Examine the linguistic proof
Ghazali provides evidence from Arabic usage and poetry that 'istawa' can mean dominance, as in a ruler establishing control over a kingdom.
This shows the term is appropriate.
5
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that 'istawa' definitively means divine dominance over the Throne, an interpretation that avoids anthropomorphism while honoring the text.
This is a model of balanced theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلنرجع إلى معنى الاستواء والنزول : أما الاستواء : فهو نسبة العرش إليه لا محالة ، ولا يمكن أن يكون مناقشة معاني الاستواء والخلوص للعرش إليه نسبة إلا بكونه : معلوماً ، أو مراداً ، أو مقدوراً عليه ، أو محلاً مثل محل العرض ، أو مكاناً مثل مستقر الجسم . ولكن بعض هذه النسب تستحيل عقلاً ، وبعضها لا يصلح للاستعارة له ، فإن كان في جملة هذه النسب - مع أنه لا نسبة سواها - نسبة لا يحيلها العقل ، ولا ينبو عنها اللفظ . . فليعلم أنها المراد . أما كونه مكاناً ومحلاً كما هو للجوهر والعرض .. فاللفظ يصلح له ولكن العقل يحيله كما سبق . وأما كونه معلوماً ومراداً .. فالعقل لا يحيله ، ولكن اللفظ لا يصلح له . وأما كونه مقدوراً عليه ، وواقعاً في قبضة القدرة ومسخراتها مع أنه أعظم المخلوقات ، ويصلح الاستيلاء عليه ؛ لأن يتمدَّحَ به ، وينبه به على غيره الذي هو دونه في العظم.. فهذا مما لا يحيله العقل ، ويصلح له اللفظ ، فأخلق بأن يكون هو المراد قطعاً . أما صلاح اللفظ له .. فظاهر عند الخبير بلسان العرب ، وإنما ينبو عن مثل هذا أفهام المتطفلين على لغة العرب ، الناظرين إليها من بعد ، الملتفتين إليها التفات العرب إلى لسان الترك ، حيث لم يتعلموا منه إلا أوائله ! فمن المستحسن في اللغة أن يقال : استوى الأمير على مملكته ؛ حتى قال الشاعر : [من الرجز] قَدِ اسْتَوى بِشَرٌ عَلَى الْعِرَاقِ مِنْ غَيْرِ سَيْفِ وَدَمٍ مِهْراقٍ ولذلك قال بعض السلف : أفهم من قوله : ( اسْتَوَى عَلَى الْعَرْشِ ) ما فهم من قوله : ( ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى إِلَى السَّمَاءِ وَهِيَ دُخَانُ )
Let us return to the meaning of istiwa and nuzul. As for istiwa: it is the relation of the Throne to Him necessarily, and it is not possible to discuss the meanings of istiwa and the attribution of the Throne to Him except by it being: known, or willed, or under power, or a locus like an accident, or a place like the location of a body. But some of these relations are rationally impossible, and some are unsuitable for metaphorical use for Him. If among these relations - and there is no other relation - there is a relation that reason does not deem impossible and language does not shrink from, then let it be known that it is intended. As for its being a place and locus as for substance and accident, the word is suitable but reason deems it impossible as previously. As for its being known and willed, reason does not deem it impossible but the word is not suitable. As for its being under power, and falling under the grasp of omnipotence and its subjugations, while it is the greatest of creatures, and it is suitable for having control over it, because one may boast of it and direct attention to something else that is lesser in greatness - this is something that reason does not deem impossible and the word is suitable for it. So it is most fitting that it is the intended meaning definitively. As for the suitability of the word for it, it is apparent to the expert in the Arabic tongue; only the understanding of those who meddle in the Arabic language from a distance, looking at it like Arabs look at the Turkish language, having learned only its beginnings, shrink from this. For it is linguistically correct to say: 'The prince established himself over his kingdom' until the poet said: 'Bishr has established himself over Iraq without sword or spilled blood.' And for that reason some of the Salaf said: 'I understand from His saying "He established over the Throne" what I understand from His saying "Then He turned to the heaven while it was smoke."'
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rational and linguistic analysis of the meaning of istiwa (establishing over the Throne). Ghazali begins by stating that istiwa necessarily involves a relation between the Throne and God. He then enumerates five possible types of relation: the Throne being known to God, being willed, being under His power, being a locus (mahall) for Him like an accident, or being a place (makan) like a body. He rules out the latter two because they imply corporeality and are rationally impossible. The first two (known and willed) are rationally possible but not suitable linguistically as meanings of istiwa because the word 'istawa' does not convey mere knowledge or will. The only one that is both rationally possible and linguistically appropriate is the third: the Throne being under God's power (maqdoor alayh) and under His control. Ghazali supports this with the Arabic usage of 'istawa' meaning to dominate or take control, as in the poet's line about Bishr establishing himself over Iraq. He then cites a Salaf (Sufyan al-Thawri) who understood istiwa in the same sense as in the verse about turning to the heaven, which indicates direction, so istiwa here means turning/commanding. Thus, the correct interpretation avoids anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
