العرش
النسبة
معلوم
مراد
مقدور عليه
محل
مكان
استعارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali denies the attribute of istiwa altogether or interprets it in a way that strips God of sovereignty.
Correction: Ghazali affirms the attribute but interprets it as divine dominance and control, consistent with God's transcendence and the Arabic language.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following relations of the Throne to God is both rationally possible and linguistically suitable for the word 'istawa'?
A) Being a locus for God.
B) Being known by God.
C) Being under God's power.
D) Being a place.

Key Takeaway
The only valid interpretation of 'istawa' that is both rationally possible and linguistically sound is that of divine dominance and control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'He established over the Throne' (Quran 7:54), does your mind automatically picture a physical sitting? What does Ghazali's analysis reveal about the need to discipline your intellect and rely on linguistic expertise?
?
Are you willing to accept an interpretation that may feel less emotionally satisfying (like 'dominance') because reason and language demand it, even if it means letting go of a more tangible image of Allah? How does this lesson challenge your attachment to literalism?
?
Ghazali uses a systematic method to arrive at the correct meaning. How often do you approach verses about Allah's attributes with such careful reasoning, or do you rely on hearsay and tradition? What step can you take today to deepen your understanding of a single attribute?

Introduction to Interpreting the Hadith of God's Descent
Ghazali introduces the hadith and its interpretability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali approaches the hadith of God's descent by opening the door for interpretation from two aspects.

Core Idea
Ghazali affirms the hadith while allowing interpretation — a balanced approach.

In this passage, Ghazali begins his discussion of the prophetic hadith about God's descent to the lowest heaven. Rather than accepting a literal reading or rejecting the hadith, he states that it has room for interpretation from two angles. This sets up a reasoned approach that upholds the text while respecting divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Ghazali is starting his analysis of the hadith and immediately introduces the concept of ta'wil.
The passage is brief but sets the stage for a detailed discussion.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms 'nuzul' (descent) and 'ta'wil' (interpretation). Understand that ta'wil is not a rejection but a method of understanding that aligns with reason and other texts.
Ghazali uses ta'wil similarly in his discussion of istiwa (seating).
3
Understand the two-aspect framework
Ghazali mentions that there is scope for interpretation from two aspects. Though these aspects are not detailed here, this indicates a structured approach. Reflect on what two aspects could be: likely linguistic and rational.
Later in the text, Ghazali will elaborate on both aspects.

This passage is the beginning of Ghazali's discussion of the hadith about God's descent (nuzul). He states that this hadith has room for interpretation (ta'wil) from two angles. Criteria for Divine Attributes: Reason and Language
Ghazali distinguishes which attributes are rationally and linguistically suitable for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses two criteria—rational possibility and linguistic appropriateness—to classify which attributes can be applied to Allah.

Core Idea
Not every rationally possible attribute is linguistically fitting for Allah.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali clarifies an important methodological point: when we speak about Allah, we must consider both what is rationally possible and what is linguistically customary. He examines four potential attributes—being a place, being known, being willed, and being under power—and evaluates them against these two criteria. This will help you see why some terms are used and others avoided in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text carefully, then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's own words set the stage for your analysis.
2
Identify the four categories and the two criteria
Notice that Ghazali divides the attributes into four groups. For each, he asks two questions: Is it rationally possible? Is the word suitable?
The two criteria are 'aql (reason) and lafz (word/language).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that only the fourth attribute—being under power—passes both tests. Ask yourself why this matters for how we describe Allah.
This shows that theological language is not arbitrary; it is constrained by both reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما كونه مكاناً ومحلاً كما هو للجوهر والعرض .. فاللفظ يصلح له ولكن العقل يحيله كما سبق
وأما كونه معلوماً ومراداً .. فالعقل لا يحيله ، ولكن اللفظ لا يصلح له . وأما كونه مقدوراً عليه ، وواقعاً في قبضة القدرة ومسخراتها (١) مع أنه أعظم المخلوقات ، ويصلح الاستيلاء عليه ؛ لأن يتمدَّحَ به ، وينبه به على غيره الذي هو دونه في العظم.. فهذا مما لا يحيله العقل ، ويصلح له اللفظ ، فأخلق بأن يكون هو المراد قطعاً
As for His being a place and locus as is the case for substance and accident... the word is suitable for it but the intellect deems it impossible as before. And as for His being known and willed... the intellect does not deem it impossible, but the word is not suitable for it. And as for His being within power (qudra), and occurring in the grasp of power and its subjugations (١), even though He is the greatest of created things, and domination over Him is suitable, because one may boast by it and draw attention by it to another which is inferior in greatness... this is something which the intellect does not deem impossible, and the word is suitable for it, so it is most fitting to be the intended meaning definitively.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage classifies divine attributes based on rational possibility and linguistic appropriateness. Ghazali examines four categories: being a place/locus, being known, being willed, and being under divine power. He states that being a place or locus is rationally impossible for Allah (as established earlier) even though the word might be used. Being known and willed are rationally possible, but the Arabic language does not customarily apply those terms to Allah in this context. Being under power (maqdoor) is both rationally possible and linguistically suitable; indeed it is praiseworthy to attribute power over all things, including the greatest, to Allah. Therefore, Ghazali concludes that this last category is definitively the intended meaning when such terms are used.

Key Terms
مكان (makaan)
محل (mahall)
معلوم (ma'loom)
مراد (muraad)
مقدور (maqdoor)
قدرة (qudra)
لفظ (lafz)
عقل (aql)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that if reason allows an attribute, it is automatically affirmed of Allah.
Correction: Ghazali shows linguistic custom also restricts what can be said; reason is not the only criterion.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which attribute is both rationally possible and linguistically appropriate for Allah?
A) Being a place
B) Being known
C) Being willed
D) Being under power

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that for an attribute to be used of Allah, it must pass both the test of reason and the test of linguistic custom; being under power is the only one that does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah, do you ever use terms that are intellectually possible but not linguistically appropriate? Why do you think Ghazali warns against that? How will you guard your tongue from now on?
?
Ghazali says that being under Allah's power (maqdoor) is praiseworthy because it directs attention to His greatness. Do you feel pride or humility when you reflect that you are wholly under His power? How does this change the way you view your own abilities?
?
Consider one attribute you use for Allah that might be rationally possible but not in line with linguistic custom (e.g., saying Allah is 'happy'). After this lesson, what will you do differently?

The Linguistic and Salafic Case for 'Istawa'
Ghazali defends 'istawa' linguistically and cites Salaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the term 'istawa' (establishing) for Allah using Arabic linguistic norms and the authority of the Salaf.

Core Idea
'Istawa' in Arabic means taking control, not physical sitting.

Ghazali begins by asserting that the linguistic suitability of 'istawa' is obvious to any expert in Arabic. Only those with superficial knowledge of the language object. To prove his point, he cites a common metaphorical usage and a poetic example, then invokes the early Muslims (Salaf) who understood the throne verse in a non‑literal sense—just as they understood another verse about heaven. This short but powerful passage anchors one of the most debated divine attributes in sound Arabic usage and early tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali moves from a general claim to a concrete example and then to a Salafic citation.
2
Understand the linguistic defence
Ghazali says the suitability of 'istawa' is clear to Arabic experts. He contrasts them with those who only dabble. Identify the attitude he criticizes.
He uses the image of Arabs looking at Turkish from afar—a strong way to say they lack real knowledge.
3
Analyze the poetic example
The line 'Bishr has established (istawa) himself over Iraq without sword or blood' shows that 'istawa' is used for taking authority, not physical sitting. Notice the absence of any physical contact—it's purely metaphorical control.
4
See how the Salaf understood it
Some Salaf said they understand 'istawa over the Throne' the same way they understand 'istawa to the heaven'—i.e., as an act of divine will or direction, not a physical movement. This shows that early Muslims did not take it literally.
The second verse (41:11) uses 'istawa ila' which means turned/directed, not sat.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Ghazali uses three layers: linguistic fit, poetic precedent, and Salafic exegesis. Together they show that 'istawa' for Allah is both proper Arabic and orthodox.
This passage is the core of his defence against anthropomorphism.

Commentary
This passage defends the linguistic appropriateness of the term 'istawa' (to establish, to settle) for Allah. Ghazali first notes that the suitability is obvious to Arabic experts; only those with superficial knowledge of Arabic would object. He then quotes a poetic line about Bishr establishing himself over Iraq, showing that the word implies taking control without physical contact. Then he invokes the authority of some Salaf (early Muslims) who said that they understand the verse about the Throne in the same sense as the verse about the heaven—i.e., not literally as physical settlement but as a divine act of will or authority. This demonstrates that the Salaf themselves did not take 'istawa' in a corporeal sense.

Metaphorical Interpretations of the Hadith of Descent
Ghazali provides two figurative interpretations for the descent hadith and evaluates three meanings of 'nuzul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses linguistic and rational arguments to interpret the hadith of descent figuratively, avoiding literal spatial motion or change in Allah.

Core Idea
Divine descent is metaphorical: it means mercy, not movement.

The hadith 'Allah descends to the lowest heaven' is a challenge for theologians: taken literally, it implies spatial change. Ghazali shows that both language and reason demand a figurative reading. In this passage, he offers two specific interpretations and then evaluates three possible meanings of 'nuzul' to identify the only intellectually sound one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on Ghazali's overall argument structure.
The text is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two respects (وجهان) in which the hadith can be interpreted figuratively. Note the analogies: 'ask the town' and 'the king descended'.
The first attribution shifts the descent to an angel; the second uses 'nuzul' for humility.
3
Analyze the three meanings of 'nuzul'
List the three meanings Ghazali distinguishes: spatial motion, loss of rank, and humble condescension. Understand why the intellect rejects the first two for Allah.
Spatial motion requires a body; loss of rank contradicts Allah's eternal perfection.
4
Review the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding. The answer is C – only humble condescension is permissible.

Discussion of the meanings of 'nuzul' (descending). And as for his saying, peace be upon him, 'Allah descends to the lowest heaven'... there is room for interpretation (ta'wil) in two respects:
First: in attributing the descent to Him, and that it is metaphorical (majaz), and in reality it is attributed to an angel from the angels; just as He said: 'And ask the town' while the one asked is actually the people of the town. This is also common in speech, meaning the attribution of the states of the follower to the followed. So it is said: 'The king descended at the city gate' meaning his army. For if someone reports the king's descent at the city gate, he might be asked: 'Why didn't you go out to visit him?' He says: 'No, because he diverged on the way to hunt and has not yet descended.' So he is not told: 'Why did you say the king descended and now you say he hasn't descended yet?' So the understanding from the king's descent is the descent of the army. This is clear and obvious.
Second: that the word 'nuzul' may be used for kindness and humility with respect to created beings, just as elevation is used for pride. It is said: 'So-and-so raised his head to the sky's canopy' meaning he became proud. And it is said: 'He ascended to the highest heights' meaning he became great. And for one whose affair is exalted, it is said: 'His affair is in the seventh heaven.' And in contrast, if his status falls, it is said: 'He has descended to the lowest depths.' And if he humbles himself and is kind, it is said: 'He has lowered himself to the ground and descended to the lowest degrees.'
If this is understood, and it is known that 'nuzul' is used for descending from a place, and for descending from rank (by abandoning it or falling from it), and for descending from rank by way of humility and the abandonment of the act required by high rank and perfect self-sufficiency... then let us look at these three meanings between which the word oscillates: which of them does the intellect allow?
As for descending by way of spatial motion... the intellect has deemed it impossible as before, for that is only possible for a being that occupies space (mutahayyiz).
As for the falling of rank... it is impossible because He, exalted, is eternal in His attributes and majesty, and it is not possible for His highness to cease.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage provides two figurative interpretations (ta'wil) for the hadith that Allah descends to the lowest heaven. The first interpretation: the descent is attributed metaphorically to Allah, but in reality it is an angel's action. Ghazali gives an analogy from the Qur'an where 'ask the town' means ask the people. Likewise, saying 'the king descended' often means his army descended. This is a well-known rhetorical device where the follower's action is attributed to the leader. The second interpretation: the word 'nuzul' can be used figuratively to mean humility and kindness, just as 'elevation' is used for pride. Ghazali then outlines three literal meanings of 'nuzul': (1) spatial movement from a higher to a lower place, (2) loss of rank or status, and (3) voluntary humble condescension. He argues that the intellect rejects the first because it requires a physical location (mutahayyiz). The second is also impossible because Allah's highness is eternal and cannot be lost. Therefore, the only intellectually permissible meaning is the third: a figurative condescension that conveys closeness and mercy without implying physical motion or change.

نزول (nuzul)
تنزل (tanazzul)
سماء دنيا (samaa' dunya)
ملائكة (malaikah)
مجاز (majaz)
تأويل (ta'wil)
رتبة (rutba)
تواضع (tawadu')
تكبر (takabbur)
متحيز (mutahayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of descent must be interpreted literally, implying that Allah physically moves to a lower place.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates that both linguistic usage and rational necessity require a figurative interpretation. Literal descent would necessitate spatial location (mutahayyiz), which is impossible for Allah. The only permissible meaning is metaphorical condescension expressing mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which meaning of 'nuzul' is possible for Allah?
A) Spatial descent from a higher place
B) Loss of rank or status
C) Humble condescension as a metaphor for mercy
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the hadith of descent must be understood metaphorically, as literal descent is rationally impossible for Allah; only the meaning of humble condescension is intellectually sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah 'descending' during the last third of the night, do you feel a physical closeness? How does this lesson challenge that instinct, and what will you do to replace that image with a sense of His mercy instead?
?
Ghazali links 'elevation' with pride and 'descent' with humility. Which stance—spiritual pride or humble condescension—characterizes your relationship with Allah? What concrete act of tawadu' can you perform today to mirror the meaning of 'nuzul'?
?
If you have ever defended a literal reading of this hadith out of fear of weakening faith, what does this lesson reveal about the true strength of your trust in Allah's transcendence? Will you now accept that figurative interpretation is not compromise but intellectual integrity?

The Meaning of Nuzul as Mercy
Ghazali explains divine descent as mercy to encourage worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali interprets the attribute of nuzul (divine descent) as a figurative expression of mercy and encouragement for worship, not as a physical movement.

Core Idea
God's 'descent' is mercy, not motion.

Ghazali addresses a key theological challenge: how can we speak of God 'descending' without implying change or limitation? In this passage, he explains that the Companions felt overwhelmed by God's majesty, so the term was used to encourage them to pray and worship. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how metaphorical interpretation preserves both transcendence and intimacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument from definition to conclusion.
Notice how Ghazali starts by defining the acceptable meaning before giving the historical context.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
Break the passage into three sections: (1) the definition of nuzul as gentleness and mercy, (2) the Companions' awe and the need for encouragement, (3) the analogy with earthly kings to illustrate the condescension of mercy.
This will help you see the logical progression.
3
Reflect on the analogy with kings
Ghazali compares God's condescension to a king allowing lowly subjects to approach. Consider why this analogy is powerful and how it supports the interpretation.
Think about why the Companions would relate to this.
4
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that the expression 'descent' is appropriate for the situation but must not be taken literally. Notice his final warning against 'the ignorant' who understand it physically.
This is the key takeaway: the term is figurative, not literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النزول بمعنى اللطف والرحمة وترك الفعل اللائق بالاستغناء وعدم المبالاة فهو ممكن، فيتعين التنزيل عليه. وقيل: إنه لما نزل قوله تعالى: (رَفِيعُ الدَّرَجَاتِ ذُو الْعَرْشِ) استشعر الصحابة منه مهابة عظيمة، واستبعدوا الانبساط في السؤال والدعاء مع ذلك الجلال، فأُخبِروا بأن الله تعالى مع عظم جلاله وعلو شأنه متلطف بعباده، رحيم بهم، مستجيب لهم، مع الاستغناء عنهم إذا دعوه. وكأنَّ استجابة الدعوة نزول بالإضافة إلى ما يقتضيه ذلك الجلال من عدم المبالاة، فعبَّر عن ذلك بالنزول تشجيعاً لقلوب العباد على المباسطة بالأدعية، بل على الركوع والسجود، فإن من يستشعر بقدر طاقته مبادي جلال الله تعالى استبعد سجوده وركوعه. فإنَّ تقرُّبَ العباد كلهم بالإضافة إلى جلال الله تعالى أخس من تحريك العبد إصبعاً من أصابعه على قصد التقرب إلى ملك من ملوك الأرض، ولو عظم به ملكاً من الملوك لاستحق به التوبيخ، بل من عادة الملوك زجر الأراذل عن الخدمة والسجود بين أيديهم والتقبيل لعتبة دورهم استحقاراً لهم عن الاستخدام، وتعاظماً عن استخدام غير الأمراء والأكابر، كما جرت به عادة بعض الخلفاء! فلولا النزول عن مقتضى الجلال باللطف والرحمة والاستجابة لاقتضى ذلك الجلال أن تبهت العقول عن الفكر، وتخرس الألسنة عن الذكر، وتخمد الجوارح عن الحركة، فمن لاحظ ذلك الجلال وهذا اللطف استبان له على القطع أن عبارة النزول مطابقة للحال، ولفظ مطلق في موضعه لكن لا على ما فهمه الجهال.
As for descent (nuzūl) in the sense of gentleness (luṭf) and mercy (raḥma), and abandoning the act befitting self-sufficiency and indifference, it is possible, so interpretation (tanzīl) to that is necessarily determined. And it was said: When the verse 'Exalted in degrees, Possessor of the Throne' descended, the Companions sensed from it great awe, and they considered it unlikely to be expansive in questioning and supplication with that majesty. So they were informed that God Almighty, despite the greatness of His majesty and the loftiness of His status, is gentle with His servants, merciful to them, responsive to them, while being self-sufficient from them when they supplicate Him. It is as if the response to supplication is a descent in addition to what that majesty requires of indifference, so He expressed that by descent to encourage the hearts of the servants to be expansive in supplications, indeed in bowing and prostrating. For whoever senses, according to his capacity, the beginnings of the majesty of God Almighty would consider his bowing and prostrating unlikely. Indeed, the drawing near of all servants in addition to the majesty of God Almighty is more contemptible than a servant moving a finger intending to draw near to a king of the earth. And if he magnified a king of the kings by that, he would deserve rebuke. Rather, it is the habit of kings to repel the lowly from service and prostration before them and kissing the threshold of their houses, considering them too contemptible for employment, and deeming themselves too great to employ other than princes and nobles, as was the custom of some caliphs! If not for the descent from what majesty requires, by gentleness, mercy, and response, that majesty would necessitate that intellects be stunned from thought, tongues be silenced from remembrance, and limbs be stilled from movement. So whoever observes that majesty and this gentleness will clearly realize that the expression 'descent' is appropriate to the situation, and an absolute word in its place, but not as the ignorant understand it.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is an interpretive explanation of the term 'nuzūl' (descent) attributed to God. Ghazali begins by clarifying that if descent is understood in a figurative sense of gentleness and mercy, it is acceptable, and interpretation must be applied accordingly. He then provides the context: when the verse 'رفيع الدرجات ذو العرش' was revealed, the Companions felt extreme awe and hesitated in prayer and supplication. To encourage them, God informed them of His gentleness and mercy despite His majesty. Ghazali explains that the response to prayer is called 'descent' metaphorically, as it contrasts with the indifference that majesty would imply. He then elaborates on the insignificance of all creation's worship compared to God's majesty—comparing it to moving a finger before an earthly king, which would be rebuked. He notes that even kings repel lowly people from service. Thus, without God's descent into mercy, His majesty would overwhelm intellect, speech, and action. The conclusion is that the term is appropriate for the situation, but not in the literal sense understood by the ignorant (the anthropomorphists).

النزول
اللطف
الرحمة
الجلال
الجهال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That nuzul implies physical movement or change in God.
Correction: Ghazali argues that nuzul is a metaphor for mercy and encouragement, not a literal descent. The term is used to address human psychological needs while preserving God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why did God use the term 'nuzul' for His response to supplication?
A) To indicate that God physically moves to a lower place.
B) To encourage the Companions and believers to pray and worship despite God's majesty.
C) To show that God is indifferent to worship.
D) To limit His mercy to the lowest heaven.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that describing God's response to prayer as 'descent' is a metaphor for mercy, not a physical movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Imagine that tonight, before you sleep, you will stand before God in prayer. If you truly believed that He is both infinitely majestic and infinitely gentle—so much so that He lowers Himself to hear you—what specific fear or excuse would you have to release to cry out to Him with complete openness?

Why the Lowest Heaven and Night: Rational Answers
Ghazali gives rational answers to two objections about nuzul's specifics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rational explanations for why divine descent is linked to the lowest heaven and nighttime.

Core Idea
God's descent is specified to extremes to indicate degree, not location.

Ghazali anticipates objections from rationalists who find the specifics of nuzul problematic. He provides two complementary answers: one based on linguistic convention about extremes, the other on the psychological suitability of night for sincere supplication. These answers show that the specifics do not imply literal location or time restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and answers
Read the Arabic passage containing two questions about specification of the lowest heaven and nights, followed by Ghazali's responses.
Notice the structure: each objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل' and the answer by 'قلنا'.
2
Analyze the first answer
Understand the analogy: 'descended to the lowest heaven' is like 'fell to the earth' – the extreme point is meant, not a literal place.
The Pleiades and earth examples show linguistic usage for highest and lowest.
3
Analyze the second answer
Night is specified because stillness and solitude make supplications more sincere and likely to be answered.
Ghazali emphasizes that heedless supplication during busy times is not the same.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how both answers protect divine transcendence while respecting the text.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فلِمَ خَصَّصَ السماء الدنيا؟ قلنا: هو عبارة عن الدرجة الأخيرة التي لا درجة بعدها؛ كما يقال: سقط إلى الثرى، وارتفع إلى الثريا، على تقدير أن الثريا أعلى الكواكب، والثرى أسفل المواضع. فإن قيل: فلم خَصَّصَ بالليالي فقال: «ينزل كل ليلة»؟ قلنا: لأن الخلوات هي مظنة استجابة الدعوات، لذلك، حيث تسكن الخلق، وينمحي عن القلوب ذكرهم، ويصفو الذكر لله تعالى، فمثل هذا الدعاء هو المرجو له الاستجابة، لا ما يصدر عن غفلة القلوب عند تزاحم الأشغال. فوجب الحذر من التعطيل الذي هو قول المعتزلة والجهمية، ومن اعتقاد حقيقة الظاهر الذي هو قول المشبهة والحشوية، فكلا القولين ذميم مبتدع، وقد بين المؤلف في رسالته المسماة «الجامع للعوام» الواجب اعتقاده على كل مسلم عند سماع أمثال هذه الآيات والأحاديث: من تقديس، وتصديق، واعتراف بالعجز، وسكوت، وكف، وإمساك، وتسليم لأهل المعرفة. والسلف رضي الله عنهم عندما سلموا وأمروها كما جاءت لم يجروها على ظاهرها قطعاً، فعدم إجرائها على الظاهر هو نوع تأويل لمن تأمَّل ذلك، فلما اختلط الناس وتمازجت معارفهم ولم يكن لكثير منهم نصيب من فهم اللغة ودلالاتها ظهر التأويل بجلاء وقد كان نتفاً يسيرة عند السلف، وهذا التأويل هو نقل هذه الظواهر من صفات الذات إلى صفات الأفعال؛ فالسلف اعتقدوا أنه سبحانه له يد منزهين غير مشبهين كما مرَّ هو أعلم بمرادها، وهم يثبتون له بذلك صفة ذات، فمن قال: اليد القدرة واللغة
If it is said: Why did He specify the lowest heaven? We say: It is an expression for the last degree after which there is no degree; just as one says: 'He fell to the earth' and 'He rose to the Pleiades,' on the assumption that the Pleiades are the highest stars and the earth is the lowest place. If it is said: Why did He specify nights, saying 'He descends every night'? We say: Because times of solitude are occasions for the answering of supplications, therefore, when creation is still, and their remembrance is effaced from hearts, and remembrance of God becomes pure, such supplication is hoped for response, not that which arises from heedlessness of hearts amid the press of occupations. So one must beware of ta‘ṭīl (divesting God of attributes), which is the saying of the Mu‘tazila and Jahmiyya, and of believing the literal external meaning, which is the saying of the Mushabbiha and Hashwiyya; both sayings are blameworthy innovations. And the author has explained in his treatise called 'al-Jāmi‘ li-l-‘Awāmm' what every Muslim must believe upon hearing such verses and hadiths: sanctification, affirmation, confession of inability, silence, restraint, withholding, and submission to the people of knowledge. The Salaf, may God be pleased with them, when they submitted and passed them on as they came, did not apply them literally at all. So not applying them literally is a kind of ta’wīl (interpretation) for one who reflects on that. When people mixed and their knowledge intermingled, and many had no share in understanding the language and its indications, ta’wīl appeared clearly, while it had been rare among the Salaf. This ta’wīl is transferring these apparent meanings from attributes of essence to attributes of actions. The Salaf believed that He, exalted, has a hand—transcendent and not likened—He is most knowing of its intent, and they thereby affirmed for Him an attribute of essence. So whoever says 'the hand is power'—and language...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section followed by a methodological conclusion. First, an objection is raised: why is the descent specified to the lowest heaven? Ghazali answers by analogy: the lowest heaven is like 'the earth' or 'the lowest place,' just as we say 'fell to the earth' or 'rose to the Pleiades'—the specification indicates the extreme degree, not literal location. The second objection asks why descent is specified to nights. The answer: nights are times of solitude and stillness, when supplication is more sincere and free from distraction. Ghazali then warns against two extremes: ta‘ṭīl (denial of attributes) of the Mu‘tazila and Jahmiyya, and tashbīh (anthropomorphism) of the Mushabbiha and Hashwiyya. He refers to his own treatise for the correct approach: sanctification, affirmation, silence, and submission to scholars. Finally, he explains that the Salaf did not take these texts literally; their non-literal acceptance is itself a form of ta’wīl (interpretation). Later, when knowledge mixed and linguistic understanding declined, explicit ta’wīl became necessary, shifting apparent attributes from essence to actions. The Salaf affirmed, for example, that God has a hand, transcendent and unlike, knowing its true meaning, and they considered it an attribute of essence. The passage ends abruptly with a mention of those who say 'hand is power' and language.

Key Terms
السماء الدنيا
الليالي
الثرى
الثريا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'descent to the lowest heaven' implies God is literally in that location, making Him limited.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies it is an expression of extreme degree, not literal spatial movement.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the descent specified to nights?
A) Because night is darker and more mysterious.
B) Because solitude at night is more conducive to sincere supplication.
C) Because angels descend at night.
D) Because the hadith says so without reason.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali rationally explains the specifications of nuzul as meaningful for human understanding without compromising divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you truly sense that you are standing before the One who is as close as the 'lowest heaven'? Or do you mechanically recite words without feeling that extreme nearness? What would change in your du'a if you genuinely believed Allah is that near right now?
?
Think of your last supplication: was your heart pure from distractions, or were you preoccupied with worldly concerns? The lesson says response is hoped for when remembrance of God becomes pure. Are you willing to carve out real solitude tonight to pray with full attention?
?
You know that Allah descends to the lowest heaven every night. But do you actually act as if the last third of the night is the most precious time for you to draw near? If not, what are you prioritizing over the opportunity to speak to the Creator without interruption?

Between Ta'til and Tashbih: The Salaf's Way
Ghazali warns against two extremes and explains the Salaf's non-literal acceptance as ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's warning against two extremes (ta'til and tashbih) and the Salaf's method of non-literal acceptance which implies a form of ta'wil.

Core Idea
The Salaf accepted apparent texts without literalism, which itself is a subtle interpretation.

After answering specific objections, Ghazali turns to the broader methodological danger: either denying attributes entirely (ta'til) or taking texts literally in an anthropomorphic way (tashbih). He then describes the Salaf's approach, which avoids both extremes by accepting the texts as they came but not applying them literally. This non-literal acceptance, he argues, is a form of ta'wil that later became explicit when confusion arose.

Guided Path
1
Read the warning passages
Read the Arabic from 'فوجب الحذر' to the end. Identify the two condemned groups and their errors.
Ghazali names Mu'tazila/Jahmiyya for ta'til and Mushabbiha/Hashwiyya for tashbih.
2
Understand the Salaf's stance
Ghazali explains that the Salaf did not take these texts literally; their acceptance without interpretation is itself a kind of ta'wil.
He says 'not applying them literally is a kind of ta'wil for one who reflects.'
3
Learn about the emergence of explicit ta'wil
When people's knowledge mixed and linguistic understanding declined, explicit ta'wil became necessary to prevent misunderstanding.
Ta'wil means transferring apparent meanings from attributes of essence to attributes of actions.
4
Reflect on the Salaf's belief about God's hand
The Salaf affirmed that God has a hand, but as a transcendent attribute of essence, not comparable to creation.
The passage ends abruptly, but the point about language is introduced.

?

God is Visible: Reconciling Vision and Directionlessness
Ghazali states the ninth claim and gives the first reason for its placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's claim that God is visible and the first reason he gives for addressing it in the context of denying direction—namely, that denying vision is entailed by denying direction, and he must show how vision can be affirmed without direction.

Core Idea
God is visible even without direction – Ghazali shows the apparent conflict can be resolved.

Ghazali now turns to the ninth claim: that God is visible, contrary to the Mu'tazila. He explains why this discussion belongs in the section on God's essence by giving two reasons. Here we examine the first reason: that denying vision follows from denying direction, so he must clarify how vision is possible without spatial location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a very concise style.
2
Identify the claim and the opponent
The passage states the ninth claim: God is visible. Ghazali contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position. Identify who the 'people of truth' are in this context.
The Mu'tazila deny the vision of God in the afterlife.
3
Focus on the first reason
Ghazali says the first reason for placing this issue here is that denying vision is necessitated by denying direction. Ask yourself: How does denying direction lead to denying vision? What is the logical connection?
This is a key point in Ash'ari theology: vision does not require direction.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why Ghazali sees this as a problem that needs solving. If the Mu'tazila argue that no direction means no vision, how can the Ash'aris affirm both? This lesson sets up the solution.
The solution will be explained in subsequent passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى التاسعة
الله عز وجل مرئي ندعي : أنَّ الله سبحانه وتعالى مرئي ، خلافاً للمعتزلة عند أهل الحق
وإنما أوردنا هذه المسألة في القطب المرسوم بالنظر في ذات الله تعالى لأمرين :
أحدهما : أن نفي الرؤية مما يلزم على نفي الجهة ؛ فأردنا أن نبين كيف يُجمع بين نفي الجهة وإثباتِ الرؤية
The ninth claim: Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is visible. We claim that Allah, glorified and exalted, is visible, contrary to the Mu'tazila according to the people of truth. And we have only brought up this issue in the section designated for considering the essence of Allah, exalted, for two reasons: One of them: that the negation of vision is necessitated by the negation of direction; so we wanted to explain how to combine the negation of direction and the affirmation of vision.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a claim and the beginning of a methodological explanation. The author, Imam al-Ghazali, begins by stating the ninth claim in his discussion: that God is visible (mar'iyy). He contrasts this with the position of the Mu'tazila, who deny the vision of God, and asserts that the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) affirm it. Then he explains why he has placed this issue in the section dealing with the essence of God. He gives two reasons; this passage covers the first reason. The first reason is that denying vision (ru'ya) is entailed by denying direction (jiha). In other words, if one denies that God has a spatial direction, one might be led to deny that He can be seen. So Ghazali wants to show how it is possible to simultaneously affirm that God has no direction and yet can be seen. This is a key issue because the Mu'tazila argued that if God is not in a direction, He cannot be seen, but the Ash'aris maintain that vision is possible without direction.

Key Terms
رؤية (vision)
جهة (direction)
نفي (negation)
إثبات (affirmation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming vision of God necessarily implies that God is in a direction, making Him spatially located.
Correction: Ghazali shows that vision does not require direction, and he will explain how to reconcile the two in subsequent discussion.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the relationship between denying direction and denying vision?
A) Denying direction necessarily leads to denying vision.
B) Denying vision necessitates denying direction.
C) Denying direction is entailed by denying vision.
D) Denying direction does not affect the issue of vision.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first reason for discussing vision in the section on God's essence is to address the logical entailment that denying direction leads to denying vision, and to show how vision can be affirmed without direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah, and are you willing to submit to the fact that His vision transcends your physical senses? Is your faith in the unseen strong enough to affirm His attributes without reducing them to human limitations?
?
Have you ever felt uncomfortable with Islamic beliefs that challenge your reason, like seeing Allah without direction? Will you strive to trust the revelation over your own intellectual discomfort?
?
If someone questioned you about the possibility of seeing Allah without a location, could you confidently respond using the reasoning in this lesson? How can you prepare yourself to defend this truth with both knowledge and humility?

The Basis of Visibility: Existence and Essence
Ghazali defines visibility as a potential property of every existent essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's visibility is based on His existence and essence in potentiality, not on any action or attribute.

Core Idea
God is visible because every existent essence is potentially visible.

Ghazali presents the second reason for the vision of God: it is rooted in His very existence and essence, not in any contingent attribute. He clarifies that this visibility is a potentiality inherent in the essence, not an actual occurrence. Using an analogy of water and wine, he illustrates that potentiality does not require actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice Ghazali's precise language about existence and essence.
2
Distinguish potentiality from actuality
Ghazali emphasizes that he means visibility in potentiality, not actuality. Understand the difference: an essence is prepared for vision unless there is an external impediment.
The analogy of water and wine helps clarify this.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy: water in the river is thirst-quenching potentially, but only actually when drunk. Similarly, God's essence is potentially visible.
The analogy shows that potentiality does not require current actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أنه تعالى عندنا مرئيٌّ ؛ لوجودِهِ ووجود ذاته ، وليس ذلك إلا لذاته، فإنه ليس لفعله ولا لصفة من الصفات، بل كلُّ موجود ذات فواجب أن يكون مرئياً، كما أنه واجب أن يكون معلوماً، ولست أعني به : أنه واجب أن يكون معلوماً ومرئياً بالفعل، بل بالقوة؛ أي : هو من حيث ذاته مستعد لأن تتعلق الرؤية به، وأنه لا مانع ولا محيل في ذاته له. فإن امتنع وجود الرؤية فلأمرٍ آخر خارج عن ذاته؛ كما تقول : الماءُ الذي في النهر مرو، والخمرُ الذي في الدَّنّ مسكر، وليس كذلك؛ فإنه يسكر ويروي عند الشرب، ولكن معناه : أنَّ ذاته مستعدة لذلك، فإذا فهمت المراد منه فالنظر في طرفين : أحدهما : في الجواز العقلي والثاني : في الوقوع الذي لا سبيل إلى دركه إلا بالشرع، ومهما دل الشرع على وقوعه فقد دلَّ لا محالة على جوازه.
And the second: He, exalted, is visible according to us because of His existence and the existence of His essence, and that is only due to His essence, for it is not due to His action nor to an attribute among the attributes, rather every existent that is an essence is necessarily visible, just as it is necessarily knowable. And I do not mean by it that it is necessarily knowable and visible in actuality, but in potentiality; that is, it is by virtue of its essence prepared for vision to be related to it, and that there is no impediment nor anything making it impossible in its essence. For if the existence of vision is prevented, it is due to something else external to its essence; as you say: the water in the river is thirst-quenching, and the wine in the jar is intoxicating, but [it is] not so; for it intoxicates and quenches when drunk, but its meaning is that its essence is prepared for that. So if you understand what is meant by it, then consider two aspects: One of them: rational possibility; and the second: occurrence, which can only be known through revelation, and whenever revelation indicates its occurrence, it necessarily indicates its possibility.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. Ghazali gives the second reason for placing the vision of God here: God is visible by virtue of His existence and essence, not by any action or attribute. He then explains that visibility is a general property of every existent essence: just as every existent is potentially knowable, so it is potentially visible. He clarifies that he means potentiality, not actuality: an essence is prepared for vision unless there is some external impediment. He gives an analogy: water in the river is thirst-quenching in potentiality, and wine in the jar is intoxicating in potentiality, though the actual effect occurs only upon drinking. Similarly, God's essence has no intrinsic impediment to being seen. Then he introduces two aspects to consider: (1) rational possibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) – whether vision of God is logically possible; and (2) actual occurrence (al-wuqu') – whether it will happen, which can only be known through revelation. He emphasizes that if revelation proves occurrence, it automatically proves possibility, since occurrence entails possibility. This sets up the rational proof that follows, though the proof itself is not included in this lesson.

Key Terms
وجود (existence)
ذات (essence)
بالقوة (in potentiality)
بالفعل (in actuality)
رؤية (vision)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that if God is visible, He must be seen now in actuality.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that visibility is in potentiality, not actuality; it is a preparedness of the essence, not an actual occurrence in this life.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the basis for God being visible?
A) Because of an action of God.
B) Because of an attribute separate from His essence.
C) Because of His existence and essence.
D) Because of a divine command.

Key Takeaway
God's visibility is a potential property inherent in His essence, not dependent on any action or attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God's essence is inherently visible, yet you are not seeing Him now. What external impediments in your own life—distractions, sins, or worldly attachments—block your perception of His presence? Will you actively remove at least one of them today?
?
The water in the river is thirst-quenching whether you drink it or not. In the same way, God's visibility is real whether you long for it or not. Does your heart truly yearn for the vision of God, or are you content to remain in the darkness of neglect? What will you do to cultivate that longing starting tonight?
?
If every existent essence is potentially visible, then you too are an existent essence. You are seen by God, and your essence is exposed before Him. How does this awareness of being seen change the way you live your next hour? Will you act as if you are in His sight?

The Two Aspects: Rational Possibility and Scriptural Occurrence
Ghazali introduces the two aspects of the vision of God: rational possibility and scriptural occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between rational possibility and scriptural occurrence regarding the vision of God, and why revelation proving occurrence implies possibility.

Core Idea
Revelation of occurrence automatically proves rational possibility.

After establishing the potential visibility of God, Ghazali directs our attention to two aspects: rational possibility and actual occurrence. While reason can determine possibility, only revelation can confirm that vision will occur. Moreover, if revelation states it occurs, that necessarily implies it is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the short Arabic passage and translation. Note the two terms: rational possibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) and occurrence (al-wuqu').
2
Distinguish the two aspects
The first aspect, rational possibility, concerns what is logically possible. The second, occurrence, concerns whether it will actually happen, which only revelation can tell us.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ghazali asserts that if revelation indicates occurrence, it necessarily indicates possibility, because occurrence cannot happen without possibility.
This is a key methodological point: occurrence entails possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أنه تعالى عندنا مرئيٌّ ؛ لوجودِهِ ووجود ذاته ، وليس ذلك إلا لذاته، فإنه ليس لفعله ولا لصفة من الصفات، بل كلُّ موجود ذات فواجب أن يكون مرئياً، كما أنه واجب أن يكون معلوماً، ولست أعني به : أنه واجب أن يكون معلوماً ومرئياً بالفعل، بل بالقوة؛ أي : هو من حيث ذاته مستعد لأن تتعلق الرؤية به، وأنه لا مانع ولا محيل في ذاته له. فإن امتنع وجود الرؤية فلأمرٍ آخر خارج عن ذاته؛ كما تقول : الماءُ الذي في النهر مرو، والخمرُ الذي في الدَّنّ مسكر، وليس كذلك؛ فإنه يسكر ويروي عند الشرب، ولكن معناه : أنَّ ذاته مستعدة لذلك، فإذا فهمت المراد منه فالنظر في طرفين : أحدهما : في الجواز العقلي والثاني : في الوقوع الذي لا سبيل إلى دركه إلا بالشرع، ومهما دل الشرع على وقوعه فقد دلَّ لا محالة على جوازه.
And the second: He, exalted, is visible according to us because of His existence and the existence of His essence, and that is only due to His essence, for it is not due to His action nor to an attribute among the attributes, rather every existent that is an essence is necessarily visible, just as it is necessarily knowable. And I do not mean by it that it is necessarily knowable and visible in actuality, but in potentiality; that is, it is by virtue of its essence prepared for vision to be related to it, and that there is no impediment nor anything making it impossible in its essence. For if the existence of vision is prevented, it is due to something else external to its essence; as you say: the water in the river is thirst-quenching, and the wine in the jar is intoxicating, but [it is] not so; for it intoxicates and quenches when drunk, but its meaning is that its essence is prepared for that. So if you understand what is meant by it, then consider two aspects: One of them: rational possibility; and the second: occurrence, which can only be known through revelation, and whenever revelation indicates its occurrence, it necessarily indicates its possibility.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. Ghazali gives the second reason for placing the vision of God here: God is visible by virtue of His existence and essence, not by any action or attribute. He then explains that visibility is a general property of every existent essence: just as every existent is potentially knowable, so it is potentially visible. He clarifies that he means potentiality, not actuality: an essence is prepared for vision unless there is some external impediment. He gives an analogy: water in the river is thirst-quenching in potentiality, and wine in the jar is intoxicating in potentiality, though the actual effect occurs only upon drinking. Similarly, God's essence has no intrinsic impediment to being seen. Then he introduces two aspects to consider: (1) rational possibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) – whether vision of God is logically possible; and (2) actual occurrence (al-wuqu') – whether it will happen, which can only be known through revelation. He emphasizes that if revelation proves occurrence, it automatically proves possibility, since occurrence entails possibility. This sets up the rational proof that follows, though the proof itself is not included in this lesson.

Key Terms
جواز عقلي (rational possibility)
وقوع (occurrence)
شرع (revelation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that revelation can prove occurrence even if something is rationally impossible.
Correction: Ghazali asserts that occurrence necessarily implies possibility, so revelation of occurrence proves both occurrence and possibility.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how can we know the occurrence of the vision of God?
A) Through reason alone.
B) Through revelation alone.
C) Through both reason and revelation.
D) Through intuition.

Key Takeaway
Rational possibility must be established first, but the actual occurrence is only known through revelation, which also confirms possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse that describes something your reason finds difficult to accept—like the vision of God in the afterlife—do you immediately try to reinterpret it or do you first submit your intellect to the text, knowing that revelation proves its possibility? What is the state of your heart in that moment?
?
If someone told you that a certain belief is rationally impossible, but scholars affirm it based on revelation, would you trust your limited reasoning or the collective understanding of the pious? Is your faith rooted more in what makes sense to you or in what Allah has revealed, even when it challenges your intellect?
?
Think of a time you rejected a religious teaching because it seemed illogical. Did you first check whether your 'logic' was actually sound, or did you assume revelation must be wrong? How can you train yourself to say, 'If revelation confirms it, then it must be possible,' even before you understand how?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Vision of God
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila proof that God's visibility would require direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazāli’s refutation of the Mu‘tazilī argument that God cannot be seen because vision requires direction.

Core Idea
Vision does not require direction—a key to understanding divine sight.

In this passage, Ghazāli tackles a central Mu‘tazilī objection: that seeing God would imply He is in a physical direction, which is impossible for the Creator. He first grants their reasoning that direction is impossible for God, but denies that vision necessitates direction. He then exposes the flaw in their argument—an unjustified generalization from physical sight—and provides counterexamples, including God’s own seeing of Himself and the mirror analogy. This lesson unpacks Ghazāli’s logical strategy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila claim that if God were seen, He would have to be in a direction from the seer, which would imply He is a body or accident—impossible for God. Therefore, God cannot be seen.
This is a syllogism with two premises.
2
See Ghazali's response
Ghazali grants that being in a direction is impossible for God, but he denies the first premise that vision requires direction. He challenges the Mu'tazila to justify this premise.
He distinguishes between the two premises of the syllogism.
3
Examine his critique of empirical generalization
Ghazali points out that the Mu'tazila base their premise solely on observation of physical objects—they have never seen anything without direction. This is not a logical necessity, but a hasty generalization.
He compares it to the anthropomorphist's argument that God must be a body because all agents we know are bodies.
4
Consider the counterexamples
Ghazali offers two counterexamples: God seeing Himself (without being in a direction from Himself) and a person seeing their own reflection in a mirror (without being in front of themselves).
These show that vision without direction is possible.
5
Conclude the refutation
The Mu'tazila argument fails because it conflates physical sight with intellectual vision. Vision is a form of knowledge that does not require spatial relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجسام والأعراض في جواز تعلق العلم بذاته وصفاته ، والرؤية نوع علم لا يوجب تعلقه بالمرئي تغير صفة ، ولا يدلُّ على الحدوث ؛ فوجبَ الحكم بها على كل موجود
تحريجة: كونه مرئياً فإن قيل : كونه مرئياً يوجبُ كونه بجهة ، وكونه بجهة يوجب كونه عرضاً يثبت له الجهة أو جوهراً ، وهو محال ؛ ونظم القياس : أنه إن كان مرئياً .. فهو بجهة من الرائي ، وهذا اللازم محال ، فالمفضي إلى الرؤية محال
قلنا : أحد الأصلين من هذا القياس مسلَّم لكم ، وهو أن هذا اللازم محال ، ولكنَّ الأصل الأوّلَ - وهو ادعاء هذا اللازم على اعتقاد الرؤية - ممنوع .
فنقول : لمَ قلتم : إنَّه إن كان مرئياً .. فهو بجهة من الرائي ؟ أعلمتموه ضرورة أم بنظر ؟
ولا سبيل إلى دعوى الضرورة ، وأما النظرُ .. فلا بد من بيانه ، ومنتهاهم : أنهم لم يروا إلى الآن شيئاً إلا وكان بجهة من الرائي مخصوصة .
فنقول : وما لم يُرَ .. فلا يحكم باستحالته ، ولو جاز هذا .. للمجسمي أن يقول : إنه تعالى جسم ؛ لأنه فاعل ، فإننا لم نر إلى الآن فاعلاً إلا جسماً ، أو يقول : إن كان فاعلاً وموجوداً . . فهو إما داخل العالم وإما خارجه ، وإما متصل وإما منفصل، ولا تخلو عنه الجهات الست ، فإنه لم نعلم موجوداً إلا وهو كذلك .. فلا فضل بينكم وبين هؤلاء !
وحاصله : يرجع إلى الحكم بأنَّ ما شوهد وعلم ينبغي ألا يعلم غيره إلا على وفقه ؛ وهو كمن يعلمُ الجسم وينكرُ العرض ، ويقول : لو كان موجوداً .. لكان يختص بحيز ويمنعُ غيره من الوجود ، بحيث هو كالجسم ومنشأ هذا : إحالة اختلاف الموجودات في حقائق الخواص مع الاشتراك في أمور عامة ، وذلك تحكُم لا أصل له ، على أن هؤلاء لا يغفل عن معارضتهم بأنَّ الله تعالى يرى نفسه ، ويرى العالم ، وهو ليس بجهة من الله تعالى يرى نفسه ولا من العالم ، فإذا جاز ذلك .. فقد بطل هذا الخيال
وهذا مما يعترفُ به أكثر المعتزلة ، ولا مخرج عنه لمن اعترف به
والعالم من غير جهة
الرؤية
ومن أنكر منهم .. فلا يقدر على إنكار رؤية الإنسان نفسَهُ في المرآة : مثال لتوضيح إمكان ومعلوم أنه ليس في مقابلة نفسِهِ ، فإن زعموا أنَّه لا يرى نفسه ، وإنما يرى صورة محاكية لصورته ، منطبعة في ظاهر المرأة انطباع النقش في الحائط .. فيقالُ : إِنَّ هذا القولَ ظاهرُ الاستحالة ؛ فإن مَنْ تباعد عن مرآة منصوبة في حائط بقدر ذراعين .. يرى صورته بعيدة عن جرم المرآة بذراعين ، وإن تباعد بثلاثة أذرع .. فكذلك ، والبعيد عن المرآة بذراعين كيف يكون منطبعاً في المرأة وسمْكُ المرآة ربما لا يزيد على سمك شعيرة ؟! فإن كانت الصورة في شيء وراء المرآة .. فهو محال ؛ إذ ليس وراء المرآة إلا جدار أو هواء أو شخص آخر وهو محجوب عنه ، وهو لا يراه ، وكذا عن يمين المرآة ، وعن يسارها ، وفوقها ، وتحتها ، وجهات المرآة ستُ ، وهو يرى صورة بعيدة المرأة بذراعين ، فلتطلب هذه الصورة من جوانب المرآة ، فحيث
عن
فكما أنه سبحانه يرى العالم بغير جهة .. جازت رؤيته سبحانه بغير جهة
Bodies and accidents concerning the permissibility of knowledge attaching to His essence and attributes, and vision (al-ru'ya) is a type of knowledge that does not necessitate, by its attachment to the seen, a change in attribute, nor does it indicate temporality; thus it is necessary to judge it for every existent. Difficulty: His being seen. For if it is said: His being seen necessitates His being in a direction (jiha), and being in a direction necessitates His being an accident to which direction is established or a substance, and that is impossible. The form of the syllogism: if He is seen, then He is in a direction from the seer; this consequent is impossible, so what leads to vision is impossible. We say: one of the two premises of this syllogism is granted to you—that this consequent is impossible—but the first premise—which is claiming this consequent upon the belief in vision—is denied. So we say: Why do you say that if He is seen, then He is in a direction from the seer? Do you know this by necessity or by reasoning? There is no way to claim necessity; as for reasoning, it must be explained. Their ultimate argument is that they have not yet seen anything except that it was in a specific direction from the seer. So we say: What has not been seen is not judged impossible; and if this were permissible, then the corporealist (mujassimi) could say that God is a body because He is an agent, for we have not yet seen an agent except a body, or he could say: if He is an agent and existent, then He is either inside the world or outside it, either connected or disconnected, and the six directions are not free of Him, for we have not known any existent except that it is such. Then there would be no distinction between you and these. The upshot: it reduces to the judgment that what is seen and known requires that nothing else be known except in accordance with it; it is like one who knows the body and denies the accident, and says: if it existed, it would be specific to a space and prevent others from existence, so it is like a body. The source of this is the impossibility of differences among existents in the realities of properties while sharing in general matters, and that is an arbitrary judgment with no basis. Moreover, these people cannot ignore the objection against them that God sees Himself and sees the world, and He is not in a direction—God sees Himself and not from the world. So if that is allowed, then this imagination is invalidated. And this is something acknowledged by most of the Mu'tazila, and there is no way out for whoever acknowledges it. And the world without direction. Vision. And whoever among them denies it cannot deny a person's vision of himself in a mirror: an example to clarify possibility. And it is known that he is not in front of himself. For if they claim that he does not see himself, but rather sees an image resembling his form, imprinted on the surface of the mirror like the imprint of a carving on a wall, then it is said: This statement is evidently impossible. For if someone moves away from a mirror fixed in a wall by a distance of two cubits, he sees his image two cubits away from the body of the mirror; if he moves away three cubits, likewise. How can the image be imprinted in the mirror when its thickness is perhaps no more than a grain? If the image is in something behind the mirror, that is impossible, for behind the mirror there is only a wall, air, or another person, and he is screened from it and does not see it. Likewise to the right, left, above, and below—the directions of the mirror are six, and he sees an image two cubits away from the mirror. So let this image be sought from the sides of the mirror. Then just as He sees the world without direction, so His being seen without direction is permissible.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila argument that God cannot be seen (ru'ya) because vision requires being in a direction (jiha). Ghazali begins by stating his position: vision is a type of knowledge that does not necessitate change in the seen object nor indicate temporality; therefore, it is possible for all existents, including God. He then presents the Mu'tazila objection as a 'difficulty' (taḥrīja): if God is seen, He must be in a direction from the seer; being in a direction entails either being an accident or a substance, both impossible for God. Thus, what leads to vision is impossible. Ghazali responds by granting that the consequent (being in a direction) is impossible, but he denies the first premise that vision implies direction. He challenges the objector to justify this premise: is it known by necessity or by reasoning? Since it is not necessary, they must rely on reasoning, but their reasoning is merely empirical—they have never seen anything except in a direction. Ghazali criticizes this as a hasty generalization, analogous to the anthropomorphist (al-mujassim) who argues that God must be a body because all agents we know are bodies. This method would lead to absurdities. The root error is the arbitrary refusal to allow differences among existents while sharing general attributes. Ghazali then provides a counterexample: God sees Himself and the world without being in a direction from Himself or the world; this shows that vision without direction is possible. Most Mu'tazila acknowledge this. For those who deny it, Ghazali gives the example of a person seeing his own image in a mirror: the person is not in front of himself, yet he sees his image without being in a direction from himself. The Mu'tazila might claim it is merely an imprint on the mirror, but Ghazali refutes this by pointing out that the image appears at a distance, not on the mirror's surface, and cannot be located in any direction. Therefore, vision without direction is possible, and by analogy, God's vision of the world and His being seen are likewise possible without direction.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
جهة (jiha)
مرئي (mar'i)
محال (muḥāl)
مجسم (mujassim)
تحريجة (taḥrīja)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision always requires the seen object to be in a physical direction from the seer.
Correction: Vision is a type of knowledge that does not necessarily involve spatial relations; God's sight and intellectual vision are free from direction.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw Ghazali identifies in the Mu'tazila argument that God cannot be seen?
A) They rely on a syllogism that is logically invalid.
B) They assume that vision always requires being in a direction, based only on empirical observation of physical things.
C) They deny that God can see Himself.
D) They ignore the possibility of miracles.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the Mu'tazila argument fails because it conflates physical sight with intellectual vision, which does not require direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about seeing God, do you unconsciously imagine a physical vision? How does Ghazali's argument challenge you to purify your conception of God from human limitations? What will you do to train your mind to think of God as beyond physical categories?
?
In what areas of your life do you judge God's actions by your own limited experience, like the Mu'tazila who judged the unseen by the seen? Will you commit to trusting God's revelation even when it contradicts your empirical expectations?
?
Ghazali warns against hasty generalizations about God based on what we know of creation. Where in your faith have you assumed that God must be like something you've experienced? How can you actively resist that tendency today?

Rebuttal of the Mu'tazila's Argument from Difference to Direction
Ghazali refutes the claim that differences in existents require spatial direction, using God's self-vision as counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila's argument that differences among existents necessitate spatial direction for God, by exposing it as arbitrary and using God's self-vision as a counterexample.

Core Idea
God's seeing without direction disproves the need for spatial location.

Here Ghazali tackles a key Mu'tazila argument: if God were not spatially located, differences in existents would be impossible. He calls this arbitrary and then offers a decisive counterexample from their own doctrine—God's vision of Himself and the world without direction. This shows that difference does not require spatial direction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the first sentence carefully. The Mu'tazila argue that if God were not in a spatial location, He would be like a body—but they actually claim that the impossibility of differences among existents without spatial location forces God to have a location.
The phrase 'would be confined to a spatial location' is their conclusion.
2
Ghazali's critique
Notice that Ghazali immediately dismisses their reasoning as an arbitrary judgment (taḥakkum) with no basis. He does not engage with it at length but labels it baseless.
3
The counterexample
Ghazali then points to God's vision of Himself and the world: this occurs without any spatial direction. Since the Mu'tazila themselves accept that God sees without direction, their argument collapses.
Most Mu'tazila admit this, leaving no escape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْجُودًا .. لَكَانَ يَخْتَصُّ بِحَيِّزٍ وَيَمْنَعُ غَيْرَهُ مِنَ الْوُجُودِ، بِحَيْثُ هُوَ كَالْجِسْمِ. وَمَنْشَأُ هَذَا: إِحَالَةُ اخْتِلَافِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ فِي حَقَائِقِ الْخَوَاصِّ مَعَ الِاشْتِرَاكِ فِي أُمُورٍ عَامَّةٍ، وَذَلِكَ تَحَكُّمٌ لَا أَصْلَ لَهُ. عَلَى أَنَّ هَؤُلَاءِ لَا يَغْفُلُ عَنْ مُعَارَضَتِهِمْ بِأَنَّ اللَّهَ تَعَالَى يَرَى نَفْسَهُ، وَيَرَى الْعَالَمَ، وَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِجِهَةٍ مِنَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى يَرَى نَفْسَهُ وَلَا مِنَ الْعَالَمِ، فَإِذَا جَازَ ذَلِكَ .. فَقَدْ بَطَلَ هَذَا الْخَيَالُ. وَهَذَا مِمَّا يَعْتَرِفُ بِهِ أَكْثَرُ الْمُعْتَزِلَةِ، وَلَا مَخْرَجَ عَنْهُ لِمَنِ اعْتَرَفَ بِهِ.
If it were existent, it would be confined to a spatial location and prevent other than it from existence, such that it would be like a body. The origin of this is the impossibility of differences among existents in the realities of properties while they share in general matters, and that is an arbitrary judgment with no basis. Moreover, these people should not neglect objecting to them that God the Exalted sees Himself and sees the world, and He is without direction—God the Exalted sees Himself and not from the world. So if that is permissible, then this imagination has become invalid. And this is what most of the Mu'tazila acknowledge, and there is no way out of it for whoever acknowledges it.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument used by the Mu'tazila. Ghazali begins by presenting the Mu'tazila's claim: if a single existent (like the divine essence) were not confined to a spatial location (ḥayyiz), then differences among existents in their properties would be impossible while they share general attributes. They conclude that God must be like a body (jism) in having location. Ghazali calls this an arbitrary judgment (taḥakkum) with no foundation. He then counters with a decisive objection: God sees Himself and the world without any spatial direction (jiha). Since this is accepted, the original argument collapses. Furthermore, Ghazali notes that most Mu'tazila admit this difficulty and cannot escape it once they concede that God sees without direction.

Key Terms
حَيِّز (spatial location)
جِهَة (direction)
تَحَكُّم (arbitrary judgment)
رُؤْيَة (vision)
مُعْتَزِلَة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali concedes the Mu'tazila premise that difference requires direction.
Correction: Ghazali does not concede; he uses their own admission of God's vision without direction to undermine their argument.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali use as the primary counterexample to the Mu'tazila argument that differences in existents require spatial direction?
A) The existence of abstract concepts
B) God's vision of Himself and the world without direction
C) The impossibility of multiple bodies in one place
D) The physical laws of optics

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila's argument by showing that their own doctrine of God's vision without direction undermines their claim that difference requires spatial location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila held an inconsistent belief—they admitted God sees without direction yet argued He must have location. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that contradict each other? What would it take for you to resolve them?
?
Ghazali calls the Mu'tazila's reasoning 'an arbitrary judgment with no basis.' Have you ever built a conviction about God on an assumption you never examined? What would it mean for you to root your faith in clear evidence instead?

Ghazali's Second Method: The Mu'tazila Misunderstand Ru'ya
Ghazali argues that the opponent's denial is due to misunderstanding ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the Mu'tazila's denial of divine vision stems from a misunderstanding of what 'ru'ya' means in the Sunni tradition, not from rational impossibility.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny God's vision because they mistake it for physical sight.

Ghazali introduces his second method for refuting the Mu'tazila's rejection of seeing God. He argues that the entire dispute hinges on a definitional error: the opponents assume that 'vision' must mean the same as seeing physical objects. This passage clarifies that the real point of contention is not reason but comprehension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms Ghazali uses: kashf, maslak, ru'ya, tahqiq.
2
Identify the opponent's mistake
Ghazali says the opponent denied ru'ya because he did not understand what Ahl al-Sunna mean by it. Identify what the opponent mistakenly thought Ahl al-Sunna intend.
The opponent equates ru'ya with seeing bodies and colors.
3
Reflect on the method's name
Ghazali calls this method 'the comprehensive uncovering' (al-kashf al-bālig). Think about why this name fits the argument that the disagreement is about definition, not rational proof.
Uncovering the misconception reveals the root of the denial.

Source Text

Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage introduces the second method (maslak thānī) for disproving the Mu'tazila's denial of God's vision. Ghazali calls it 'the comprehensive uncovering' (al-kashf al-bālig). The core argument is that the Mu'tazila misunderstand what Ahl al-Sunna mean by ru'ya (vision). The opponent assumes that ru'ya must be like ordinary vision of physical bodies and colors, i.e., requiring spatial relations and corporeal conditions. But Ghazali clarifies that the reality (ḥaqīqa) of ru'ya in the divine context is not the same as seeing physical objects. The Mu'tazila, not grasping this distinction, reject it outright. The problem is one of definition and comprehension, not of rational impossibility.

Correction: Ghazali shows that the denial is based on a false analogy: assuming ru'ya in God must be like physical sight.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila reject God's vision because they impose the conditions of physical sight on a non-physical reality.
