The Objection of Prophet Musa's Request and the Answer
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila using Musa's request and answers the objection that it proves vision impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali uses Prophet Musa's request to argue for the possibility of seeing Allah and refute the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Prophet Musa's request proves vision is possible; otherwise, the prophet would be ignorantly mistaken.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali presents a powerful argument based on the request of Prophet Musa to see Allah. The Mu'tazila had claimed that Allah cannot be seen, but Ghazali shows that this would imply ignorance on the part of a prophet who spoke directly to Allah. He then answers an objection from the Quranic verses that seem to negate vision. The argument is sharp and forces the opponent into a dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a feel for the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the rhetorical questions Ghazali uses.
2
Identify the dilemma
Ghazali presents a dilemma: either the prophet was ignorant (which would be takfir) or the Mu'tazila are wrong about vision being impossible.
3
Analyze the objection from the Quran
The opponent objects using verses 'lan taranee' and 'la tudrikuhu al-absar'. Notice how Ghazali begins to answer this objection.
The answer is cut off; consider what he might have said.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this argument is considered strong: it pits the authority of the prophet against the rational claims of the Mu'tazila.
This shows the balance between revelation and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال نبي الله موسى ومن أقوى ما يدلُّ عليه : قول موسى عليه السلام : ( أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ) عليه السلام الرؤية فإنه يستحيل أن يخفى على نبي من أنبياء الله تعالى انتهى منصبه إلى أن يكلمه الله سبحانه شفاهاً ، فيجهل من صفات ذاته تعالى ما عرفه المعتزلة (۱) ! هذا معلوم على الضرورة بطلانه ؛ فإنّ الجهل بكونه ممتنع الرؤية عند الخصم . . يوجب التكفير أو التضليل ، وهو جهل بصفة ذاته ؛ لأن استحالته لذاته ، ولأنه ليس بجهة ، فكيف لم يعرف موسى عليه السلام أنه ليس بجهة ؟ أو كيف عرف أنه ليس بجهة ولم يعرف أن رؤية ما ليس بجهة عندهم محال ؟! فليت شعري ؛ ماذا يضمرُ الخصم ويقدرُهُ من ذهول موسى عليه السلام ؟ أيقدرُهُ معتقداً أنه جسمٌ في جهة ذو لون ، واتهام الأنبياء بذلك كفر إما أن نقول بجهل صريح النبي أو جهل ؛ فإنه تكفير للنبي ؛ فإن القائل بأن الله سبحانه جسم وعابد الوثن المعتزلي والشمس واحد! أو يقولُ : علم استحالة كونه بجهة ، ولكنه لم يعلم أن ما ليس بجهة فلا يرى! وهو تجهيل للنبي ؛ لأن الخصم يعتقد أنَّ ذلك من الجليات ، لا من النظريات فأنت الآن - أيها المسترشد - مخيّر بين أن تميل إلى تجهيل النبي ، أو إلى تجهيل المعتزلي ، فاختر لنفسك ما هو أليق بك ، والسلام (۱) فإن قيل : إن دل هذا لكم .. فقد دلَّ عليكم سؤاله الرؤية في الدنيا ، تحريجة: فإن كان النبي لا يجهل .. ودلّ عليكم قوله تعالى : ( لَن تَرَينِي ) وقوله سبحانه : ( لَا تُدْرِكُهُ فكيف سألها في الأَبْصَرُ الدنيا؟ وما قولكم في : ولن ترتنين) وهو لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ؟ قلنا : أما سؤال الرؤية في الدنيا .. فهو دليل على عدم معرفته بوقوع
The question of Prophet Musa - and among the strongest evidence for it is the saying of Musa (peace be upon him): 'Show me, that I may look at You.' It is impossible that a prophet of Allah who attained the rank of speaking to Allah directly should be ignorant of His attributes in a way that even the Mu'tazila know! This is known necessarily to be false; for ignorance of His being impossible to see (according to the opponent) necessitates takfir or tadlil, and it is ignorance of His essence, because its impossibility is due to His essence, and because He is not in a direction. So how did Musa not know that He is not in a direction? Or how did he know that He is not in a direction but did not know that seeing what is not in a direction is impossible according to them? I wonder: what does the opponent presume about Musa's heedlessness? Does he presume him believing that He is a body in a direction with a color? Accusing prophets of that is kufr. Either we say the prophet is ignorant or we say he is ignorant; that would be takfir of the prophet. For the one who says that Allah is a body and the idol-worshipper and the Mu'tazili worshipper of the sun are one! Or he says: he knew the impossibility of His being in a direction, but he did not know that what is not in a direction cannot be seen! That is a tadjil of the prophet, because the opponent believes that is among the self-evident matters, not theoretical. So now - O seeker of guidance - you are given a choice between inclining toward tadjil of the prophet or tadjil of the Mu'tazili; choose for yourself what is more fitting for you. And peace. If it is said: if this proves for you, then it proves against you his asking for vision in this world, as a mistake: for if the prophet is not ignorant, then the verse 'You will not see Me' and His saying 'Vision perceives Him not' prove against you. So how did he ask for it in this world? And what do you say about 'You will not see Me' and 'Vision perceives Him not'? We say: as for the request for vision in this world, it is evidence of his not knowing of its occurrence...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful argument against the Mu'tazila regarding the vision of Allah. Ghazali uses the example of Prophet Musa asking to see Allah. The Mu'tazila claim that vision is impossible, but then they must explain why Musa, who spoke to Allah, would ask for something impossible. Ghazali argues that it is impossible for a prophet of such rank to be ignorant of Allah's attributes to the extent that the Mu'tazila claim. If Musa believed vision was impossible, he would be ignorant of a self-evident attribute, which is absurd. The opponent either accuses Musa of ignorance (which is takfir) or admits that vision is possible. Then Ghazali presents an objection: if Musa's request proves vision possible, the verses 'lan taranee' and 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' prove it impossible. Ghazali begins to answer that the request in this world does not prove vision occurs in this world; it indicates he did not know that it would not occur in this world. The answer is cut off at the end of the page.

سؤال (request)
الجهل (ignorance)
التكفير (takfir)
التجهيل (tadjil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Musa's request proves vision is impossible because Allah said 'lan taranee'.
Correction: Ghazali explains that the request was for this specific world and does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Checkpoint

A) That Musa was a normal human being
B) That it amounts to takfir (declaring the prophet an unbeliever)
C) That it is a minor mistake
D) That it proves the Mu'tazila are correct

Key Takeaway
Prophet Musa's request to see Allah, when interpreted correctly, supports the possibility of vision and refutes the Mu'tazila claim of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refutation of 'Lan Tarani' Denial
Explains that 'lan tarani' was a response to Moses' request in this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's statement 'lan tarani' to Prophet Moses was a response to a request in this world, not the afterlife, and thus does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
‘Lan tarani’ was about this world, not forever.

The Mu'tazila often cite the verse 'lan tarani' (You will not see Me) from Surah al-A'raf to argue that Allah cannot be seen. However, a careful reading of the context reveals a crucial point: Prophet Moses made his request in this world, not the afterlife. Imam al-Ghazali explains that the response is specific to that situation and does not preclude ru'ya in the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the typo 'لن تركني' is corrected to 'لن تراني'.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage emphasizes that Moses sought vision 'al-haal' (in the present), not in the afterlife. This is the core point.
3
Understand the implications
The response is specific to Moses and to this world; it does not prove impossibility or apply universally.
If Moses had asked for the afterlife, the verse would be evidence against ru'ya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : لن تركني .. فهو دفع لما التمسه (۳) ، وإنما التمس ) الحال ، لا في الآخرة ، فلو قال : أرني أنظر إليك في الآخرة ، فقال : لن تراني.. لكانَ ذلك دليلاً على نفي الرؤية ، ولكن في حق موسى على الخصوص ، لا على العموم، وما كان دليلاً على الاستحالة أيضاً ؛ فكيف وهو جواب عن السؤال في الحال ؟!
وأما قوله تعالى : ( لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ) .. أي : لا تحيط به ، ولا تكتنفه من جوانبه ؛ كما تحيط الرؤية بالأجسام ، وذلك حق ، أو هو عام ، فأريد به في الدنيا ، وذلك أيضاً حق ، وهو ما أراده بقوله سبحانه : لن تركي) في الدنيا ولنقتصر على هذا القدْرِ من مسألة الرؤية ، ولينظر المنصفُ كيف
And as for His statement, Exalted is He: 'You will not see Me' — it is a repulsion of what he (Moses) sought, and he only sought (vision) in the present, not in the afterlife. For if he had said: 'Show me (Yourself) so that I may look at You in the afterlife,' and He said: 'You will not see Me,' then that would have been evidence for denying vision, but (it is) specifically concerning Moses, not universally, and it was also not evidence for impossibility; so how (could it be) when it is an answer to the question about the present? And as for His statement, Exalted is He: 'Sight does not comprehend Him' — meaning: it does not encompass Him, nor surround Him from all sides, as vision encompasses bodies; and that is true. Or it is general, but it is intended to refer to this world, and that is also true, and that is what He meant by His statement, Glorified be He: 'You will not see Me' in this world. And we will limit ourselves to this amount from the issue of ru'ya (vision), and let the fair-minded person see how
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author first addresses the verse 'lan tarani' (You will not see Me) from Surah al-A'raf (7:143). He argues that this was a response to Prophet Moses's request for vision in the present world, not in the afterlife. Therefore, it does not negate vision in the afterlife. If Moses had asked for vision in the afterlife and Allah replied 'lan tarani', that would be a proof of denial, but since the request was for the present, it is not a proof of impossibility. Moreover, even that response was specific to Moses, not a general ruling. The author then turns to the verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' (Sight does not comprehend Him, 6:103). He explains that 'idrak' means encompassing, not seeing. The verse means that vision does not encompass Allah entirely, as it does bodies. This is true. Alternatively, if the verse is taken as general, it can be interpreted as referring to this world only, which is also consistent with the previous verse. The author concludes by limiting the discussion on ru'ya to this extent, inviting the reader to reflect on how the sects have diverged.

Key Terms
رؤية
لن تراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'lan tarani' is a universal and absolute denial of the possibility of seeing Allah, even in the afterlife.
Correction: The passage shows that the verse was a response to Moses' request in this world, not a statement about the afterlife. It is specific to that context and does not prove impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does 'lan tarani' not prove that Allah cannot be seen in the afterlife?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because Moses asked to see Allah in this world, not the afterlife.
C) Because the verse only applies to Jews.
D) Because the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
‘Lan tarani’ was a response to a worldly request and does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows how we can misinterpret a verse by ignoring context. Think of a time when you took a verse out of context and derived a wrong conclusion. How will you ensure you don't repeat that mistake?
?
The desire to see Allah is a profound longing. Do you truly yearn to see your Lord in the afterlife, or have you become content with less? What changes do you need to make in your life to cultivate that sincere desire?
?
Moses, a great prophet, asked to see Allah, yet was denied in this world. How does this humble your own expectations of drawing near to Allah? Are you willing to accept Allah's timing and wisdom even when your deepest wishes are not fulfilled immediately?

Refutation of 'La Tudrikuhu al-Absar' Argument
Explains that 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' refers to encompassing, not seeing, and applies to this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' means that sight does not encompass Allah, not that it cannot see Him, and that it applies to this world, not the afterlife.

Core Idea
‘Idrak’ means encompassing, not seeing.

Another verse used by the Mu'tazila is 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' (Sight does not comprehend Him). Imam al-Ghazali clarifies that the Arabic word 'idrak' here means encompassing, not merely seeing. The verse is either a statement of truth that vision does not encompass Allah fully, or it refers to this world only, consistent with 'lan tarani'. Thus it does not deny ru'ya in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage and the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the term 'idrak' used here.
2
Understand the meaning of 'idrak'
The author explains that 'idrak' means encompassing, not merely seeing. Vision can see without encompassing.
3
Consider the two interpretations
The verse is either (a) a true statement that vision does not encompass Allah, or (b) a general statement intended for this world only. Both interpretations are consistent with affirming ru'ya in the afterlife.
Thus the verse does not contradict the possibility of seeing Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : لن تركني .. فهو دفع لما التمسه (۳) ، وإنما التمس ) الحال ، لا في الآخرة ، فلو قال : أرني أنظر إليك في الآخرة ، فقال : لن تراني.. لكانَ ذلك دليلاً على نفي الرؤية ، ولكن في حق موسى على الخصوص ، لا على العموم، وما كان دليلاً على الاستحالة أيضاً ؛ فكيف وهو جواب عن السؤال في الحال ؟!
وأما قوله تعالى : ( لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ) .. أي : لا تحيط به ، ولا تكتنفه من جوانبه ؛ كما تحيط الرؤية بالأجسام ، وذلك حق ، أو هو عام ، فأريد به في الدنيا ، وذلك أيضاً حق ، وهو ما أراده بقوله سبحانه : لن تركي) في الدنيا ولنقتصر على هذا القدْرِ من مسألة الرؤية ، ولينظر المنصفُ كيف
And as for His statement, Exalted is He: 'You will not see Me' — it is a repulsion of what he (Moses) sought, and he only sought (vision) in the present, not in the afterlife. For if he had said: 'Show me (Yourself) so that I may look at You in the afterlife,' and He said: 'You will not see Me,' then that would have been evidence for denying vision, but (it is) specifically concerning Moses, not universally, and it was also not evidence for impossibility; so how (could it be) when it is an answer to the question about the present? And as for His statement, Exalted is He: 'Sight does not comprehend Him' — meaning: it does not encompass Him, nor surround Him from all sides, as vision encompasses bodies; and that is true. Or it is general, but it is intended to refer to this world, and that is also true, and that is what He meant by His statement, Glorified be He: 'You will not see Me' in this world. And we will limit ourselves to this amount from the issue of ru'ya (vision), and let the fair-minded person see how
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author first addresses the verse 'lan tarani' (You will not see Me) from Surah al-A'raf (7:143). He argues that this was a response to Prophet Moses's request for vision in the present world, not in the afterlife. Therefore, it does not negate vision in the afterlife. If Moses had asked for vision in the afterlife and Allah replied 'lan tarani', that would be a proof of denial, but since the request was for the present, it is not a proof of impossibility. Moreover, even that response was specific to Moses, not a general ruling. The author then turns to the verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' (Sight does not comprehend Him, 6:103). He explains that 'idrak' means encompassing, not seeing. The verse means that vision does not encompass Allah entirely, as it does bodies. This is true. Alternatively, if the verse is taken as general, it can be interpreted as referring to this world only, which is also consistent with the previous verse. The author concludes by limiting the discussion on ru'ya to this extent, inviting the reader to reflect on how the sects have diverged.

Key Terms
لا تدركه الأبصار
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' proves that Allah cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The verse means that sight does not encompass Allah, not that it cannot see Him. It either states a truth about encompassing or refers to this world only.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the correct interpretation of 'idrak' in the verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar'?
A) It means vision encompasses Allah.
B) It means vision does not encompass Allah.
C) It means vision is impossible.
D) It means vision is limited to this world.

Key Takeaway
‘La tudrikuhu al-absar’ means sight does not encompass Allah, not that it cannot see Him, and it applies to this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar', do you instinctively assume it limits Allah, or do you pause to consider what 'idrak' truly means? How many times have you limited Allah because you rushed to a surface-level understanding?
?
The author invites the 'fair-minded person' to reflect. Are you fair-minded when it comes to verses that challenge your comfort zone, or do you cling to interpretations that make you feel safe, even if they might be wrong? What will you do today to ensure you are not unjust to Allah's words?
?
If you genuinely believed that you will see Allah with your own eyes in Jannah, would your longing for Jannah increase? Would it change how you live this very day? What are you willing to sacrifice to guarantee that vision?

Sunni Middle Position on Ru'ya Between Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila
Ghazali critiques Mu'tazila and Hashwiyya, then presents Sunni position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunna affirm the vision of Allah while negating direction, avoiding the extremes of the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Vision of Allah is affirmed without direction, just as knowledge is affirmed without change.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali contrasts the errors of two sectarian extremes regarding the vision of Allah. The Mu'tazila denied vision to avoid direction, while the Hashwiyya affirmed direction and fell into anthropomorphism. Ahl al-Sunna take the middle path: they affirm vision based on its similarity to knowledge, while negating direction because it depends on corporeality. Let's explore this balanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extremes
Read the passage and note how Ghazali describes the positions of the Mu'tazila and the Hashwiyya.
The Mu'tazila went to excess in tanzih; the Hashwiyya went to excess in tashbih.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each extreme
Mu'tazila thought vision implies direction, so they denied vision. Hashwiyya thought direction is necessary to affirm attributes, so they affirmed direction.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Direction is a necessary concomitant of corporeality; vision is a counterpart of knowledge. Since Allah is not a body, direction is negated. Since Allah knows, vision can be affirmed without imperfection.
Knowledge does not change the known; similarly, vision does not change the seen.
4
Recognize the parallel between vision and knowledge
Ghazali argues that vision shares with knowledge the property of not causing change in the object. This allows affirming vision without direction.
5
Appreciate the moderation
The passage concludes that this is true iqtisad (moderation) in belief, balancing between excess and deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فإنهم نفوا الجهة ، ولم يتمكنوا من إثبات الرؤية دونها
وخالفوا به قواطع الشرع ، وظنُّوا أن في إثباتها إثبات الجهة وهؤلاء تغلغلوا في التنزيه محترزين من التشبيه .. فأفرطوا ، والحشوية أثبتوا الجهة احترازاً من التعطيل .. فشبَّهوا ، فوفَّقَ الله تعالى أهل السنة للقيام بالحقِّ .. فتفطنوا للمسلك القصد (۱) ، وعرفوا أن الجهة منفية ؛ لأنها للجسمية تابعة وتتمة ، وأنَّ الرؤية ثابتةٌ ؛ لأنها رديف العلم وقريبه وهي له تكملة ، فانتفاء الجسمية أوجب انتفاء الجهة التي هي من لوازمها ، وثبوت العلم أوجب ثبوت الرؤية التي هي من روادفه أو تكملاته ، ومشاركةً له في خاصيتها ؛ وهي أنها لا توجب تغيراً في ذات المرئي ، بل تتعلق به على ما هو عليه كالعلم
ولا يخفى على عاقل أنَّ هذا هو الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد
And as for the Mu'tazila: they negated direction (al-jiha), and they were unable to affirm vision (al-ru'ya) without it. And they contradicted with that the decisive proofs of the Shari'a, and they thought that affirming vision entails affirming direction. These delved deep into transcendence (al-tanzih), guarding against anthropomorphism (al-tashbih), so they went to excess. And the Hashwiyya affirmed direction as a precaution against denial of attributes (al-ta'til), so they anthropomorphized. So Allah, Exalted is He, granted success to Ahl al-Sunna (the people of the Sunna) to uphold the truth. So they perceived the straight path, and they knew that direction is negated because it is a consequence and completion of corporeality (al-jismiyya), and that vision is affirmed because it is a counterpart of knowledge (al-'ilm) and its close associate, and a completion for it. So the negation of corporeality necessitated the negation of direction, which is one of its necessary concomitants, and the affirmation of knowledge necessitated the affirmation of vision, which is one of its correlates or completions, and it shares with it its special property: that it does not cause a change in the essence of the seen object, but rather relates to it as it is, like knowledge. And it is not hidden from any intelligent person that this is the moderation in belief.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni middle position on the issue of seeing Allah (ru'ya) by critiquing the extremes of two sects: the Mu'tazila and the Hashwiyya. The author first describes the Mu'tazila: they negated direction (jiha) from Allah, but they could not affirm vision without it, so they denied vision altogether, contradicting decisive scriptural proofs. They thought affirming vision would entail affirming direction, so they went to excess in transcendence (tanzih) to avoid anthropomorphism (tashbih). The Hashwiyya, on the other hand, affirmed direction to avoid denial of attributes (ta'til), but fell into anthropomorphism by affirming direction. The Ahl al-Sunna were guided to the moderate path. They understood that direction is negated because it is a necessary concomitant of corporeality, and Allah is not a body. Thus, direction is negated. Vision, however, is affirmed because it is a counterpart (radif) of knowledge ('ilm) and its completion (takmila). Just as knowledge does not change the known object, vision does not change the seen object. Therefore, vision can be affirmed without any imperfection. The author concludes that this balanced position is true moderation ('iqtisad) in belief.

Key Terms

جهة
جسمية
علم
تشبيه
تعطيل
تنزيه
الحشوية
المعتزلة
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming vision of Allah necessarily implies directionality or corporeality.
Correction: Vision can be affirmed without direction because direction is a concomitant of corporeality, which is negated for Allah. Vision is like knowledge: it relates to the object without requiring direction or causing change.

Checkpoint
What is the reason given that vision cannot entail direction?
A) Vision is a sense that requires direction.
B) Direction is a necessary concomitant of corporeality, which is negated for Allah.
C) The Mu'tazila proved that vision needs direction.
D) The Hashwiyya proved that direction is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni middle path affirms vision without direction by analogizing vision to knowledge, which relates without change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila were so afraid of comparing Allah to creation that they denied what the Shari'a affirms. Are there any commands or attributes of Allah that you find uncomfortable or are tempted to explain away because they don't fit your sense of 'proper' transcendence? Name one. Will you accept it as is, or will you stand with Allah's revelation over your own caution?
?
Ghazali calls the Sunni position 'the moderation in belief' (al-iqtisad fi al-i'tiqad). In your own heart, do you tend more towards excessive caution (denying what is affirmed) or excessive boldness (affirming without limit)? Give a concrete example from your life this week. What would it look like to truly follow the middle path in that specific case?

The Threefold Meaning of 'One' When Applied to Allah
Ghazali defines the three senses in which Allah is called 'one'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct senses in which Allah is called 'one' according to Imam al-Ghazali: indivisible, having no peer, and having no opposite.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness is not just numerical but also qualitative: indivisible, peerless, and without opposite.

Al-Ghazali introduces the tenth claim that Allah is one. He clarifies that this oneness is not a positive attribute but a negation of otherness. Before reading his definitions, note that he distinguishes three meanings of 'one' that protect against anthropomorphic or polytheistic misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Ghazali first states the claim, then explains each meaning.
Pay attention to the structure: claim, then three meanings.
2
Identify the three meanings
List the three senses: (1) indivisible (no quantity), (2) no peer in rank, (3) no opposite. Understand each definition.
3
Reflect on the negative aspect
Recognize that each meaning is a negation (salb) rather than an affirmation. Why does al-Ghazali emphasize this?
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى العاشرة: أنَّ الله تعالى واحد، فإن كونه واحداً يرجع إلى ثبوت ذاته ونفي ذاته وصفاته وأفعاله غيره، فليس هو نظراً في صفة زائدة على الذات؛ فوجب ذكره في هذا القطب، فنقول: الواحد قد يطلق ويراد به أنه لا يقبل الانقسام؛ أي: لا كمية له، ولا حدَّ، ولا مقدار، والباري سبحانه وتعالى واحد بمعنى: سلب الكمية المصححة للقسمة عنه، فإنه غير قابل للانقسام؛ إذ الانقسام فيما له كمية، والتقسيم تصرُّفٌ في كمّية بالتفريق والتصغير، وما لا كمية له لا يتصور انقسامه. وقد يطلق والمراد به أنه لا نظير له في رتبته؛ كما تقول: الشمس واحدة، والباري تعالى أيضاً بهذا المعنى واحد؛ فإنه لا ند له. فأما أنه لا ضدّ له .. فظاهرُ؛ إذ المفهوم من الضد: هو الذي يتعاقب الشيء على محلّ واحد ولا يجامعه، وما لا محل له فلا ضد له، والباري تعالى لا محل له .. فلا ضدَّ له. وأما قولنا: لا ند له .. فنعني به: أن ما سواه هو خالقه لا غير.
The tenth claim: that Allah Most High is one. For His being one refers to the affirmation of His essence and the negation of what is other than Him in essence, attributes, and acts. So it is not a consideration of an attribute additional to the essence; therefore it must be mentioned in this section. So we say: 'One' may be used and intended by it that it does not admit division; i.e., it has no quantity, no limit, and no magnitude. The Creator, glorified and exalted, is one in the sense of negating from Him the quantity that makes division possible, for He is not divisible; since division pertains to what has quantity, and dividing is a manipulation of quantity by separation and reduction, and that which has no quantity cannot be conceived to be divided. And it may be used, and intended by it that He has no equal in His rank; as you say: the sun is one, and the Creator exalted is also one in this sense; for He has no equivalent. As for His having no opposite, it is apparent; for the meaning of 'opposite' is that which alternates with a thing upon a single locus and does not coexist with it, and that which has no locus has no opposite, and the Creator exalted has no locus, so He has no opposite. And as for our saying: He has no equivalent, we mean by it that what is other than Him is His creation, nothing else.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'one' (al-wāḥid) as applied to Allah. Al-Ghazali begins by stating the tenth claim: that Allah is one, and he clarifies that this oneness is a negation of otherness, not an affirmative attribute. He then divides the meanings of 'one' into three senses. First, 'one' can mean indivisible, i.e., having no quantity, limit, or magnitude. Allah is one in this sense because He is not subject to division, which is only possible for things with quantity. Second, 'one' can mean having no peer in one's rank, like the uniqueness of the sun. Allah is one in this sense because He has no equal (nidd). Third, 'one' can mean having no opposite (ḍidd), which is obvious because opposites require a locus to alternate upon, and Allah has no locus. He then clarifies that saying 'no equivalent' (lā nidda) means that everything other than Him is His creation.

انقسام
كمية
نظير
ند
ضد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'one' only means numerically singular, i.e., that Allah is one in number.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that it also means indivisible, peerless, and without opposite, which are negative attributes, not positive.

Checkpoint

B) Two senses
C) Three senses
D) Four senses

Key Takeaway
Allah's oneness is understood through three negations: He is not divisible, has no equal, and has no opposite.

Refutation of Two All-Powerful Gods
Ghazali argues against two gods sharing power over all substances due to their similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that two gods cannot both have power over all things because their powers would be identical and thus redundant.

Core Idea
Two all-powerful gods would have identical power over all things, making them redundant.

Ghazali presents a sophisticated argument against the possibility of two gods each possessing absolute power. He starts from the similarity of atoms and shows that if one god's power extends to one atom, it must extend to all similar atoms. This leads to both gods having identical ranges of power, which is absurd. Let's examine the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thesis
The passage argues against the idea of two gods both being all-powerful. Ghazali aims to show logical impossibility.
2
Follow the premise
Atoms (jawahir) and their modes (akwan) are similar. The one capable of a thing is capable of its like. So power over one atom implies power over all atoms.
3
Trace the consequence
Since both powers are eternal and not restricted to a single object, they must extend to all possible atoms without limit.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Both gods would have identical power over everything, leading to redundancy and contradiction. This disproves the idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يكن قادراً عليه .. فهو محال ؛ لأن الجواهر متماثلة ، وأكوانها اختصاصات بالأحياز متماثلة ، والقادرُ على الشيء قادر على مثله التي هي إذا كانت قدرته قديمةً ، بحيث يجوز أن تتعلق بمقدورين ، وقدرة كل واحد منهما تتعلق بعدة من الأجسام والجواهر ، فلم تتقيد بمقدور واحد ، وإذا جاوز المقدور الواحد على خلاف القدرة الحادثة.. لم يكن بعض الأعداد بأولى من بعض، بل يجب الحكم بنفي النهاية عن مقدوراته ؛ فيدخل كلُّ جوهر ممكن وجوده في قدرته
And if he were not capable of it, it is impossible; because atoms (al-jawahir) are similar, and their modes (akwan) as particularizations in places are similar, and the one who is capable of a thing is capable of its like. Since His power is eternal, such that it is possible for it to attach to two objects of power, and the power of each of them attaches to a number of bodies and atoms, so it is not restricted to a single object of power. And when it goes beyond a single object of power, unlike temporal power, some numbers are not more appropriate than others, but rather it is necessary to judge the infinity of His objects of power; so every possible atom enters into His power.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the possibility of two gods each having power over all things. Ghazali begins with a conditional: if one of them were not capable of something, that thing would be impossible (because the other would have power over it, leading to contradiction). He then explains that atoms and their modes (accidents of position) are alike, and the one capable of a thing is capable of its like. Since the powers of both gods would be eternal and could attach to multiple objects, they would not be restricted to a single object. If they could exceed one object, then no number of objects would be more fitting than any other; thus, each power would have to extend to all possible atoms. This results in both gods having identical power over all things, which is absurd because it would require coordination or conflict.

Key Terms
جواهر
أكوان
قدرة قديمة
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could each be all-powerful without conflict, each controlling different domains.
Correction: Ghazali shows that their powers would necessarily extend to all atoms identically, making them redundant and logically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's first argument, what follows from the premise that atoms are similar?
A) That each god must have power over a different type of atom.
B) That power over one atom implies power over all similar atoms.
C) That two gods cannot both be all-powerful.
D) That atoms are not similar.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument shows that two all-powerful deities are logically impossible because their powers would be identical and redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Interdependence of Substances and Accidents
Ghazali presents the second division and its refutation via interdependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that two gods with separate domains (substances and accidents) would be impossible due to their mutual dependence.

Core Idea
Separate powers create mutual dependency, not harmony.

Ghazali presents a second argument against dualism: if one god controls substances and another controls accidents, they cannot act independently because substances and accidents always coexist. This interdependence leads to a logical deadlock where neither can act without the other's cooperation, rendering them powerless. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Ghazali sets up the division and then argues from interdependence.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'جواهر' (substances) and 'أعراض' (accidents). Understand that they are always found together.
This is crucial for the argument.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow Ghazali's reasoning: each creator's act depends on the other's act. This leads to mutual hindrance (تمانع).
Consider why this makes each creator incapable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثاني : أن يقال : أحدهما يقدر على الجواهر ، والآخر على الأعراض ، وهما مختلفان ، فلا يجب من القدرة على أحدهما القدرة على الآخر . وهذا محال ؛ لأن العرض لا يستغني عن الجوهر ، والجوهر لا يستغني عن العرض ؛ فيكون فعل كل واحد منهما موقوفاً على الآخر ، فإذا أراد خالق العرض خلق عرض : فكيف يخلقه وربما لا يساعده خالق الجوهر على خلق الجوهر عند إرادته لخلق العرض ؟! فيبقى عاجزاً متحيراً ، والعاجز لا يكون قادراً ، وكذا خالق الجوهر ؛ إن أراد خلق الجوهر ربما خالفه خالقُ العرض ؛ فيمتنع على الآخر خلق الجوهر ، فيؤدي إلى التمانع فإن قيل : مهما أراد أحدهما خلق جوهر . . ساعده الآخر على العرض وكذا بالعكس قلنا : هذه المساعدة : هل هي واجبة لا يتصور في العقل خلافها ، أم فإن أوجبتموها .. فهو تحكُم ، بل هو أيضاً مبطل للقدرة ؛ فإن خلق الجوهر من واحد كأنه يضطرُ الآخر إلى خلق العرض ، وكذا بالعكس ، فلا يكون له قدرة على الترك ، فلا تتحقق القدرة مع هذا وعلى الجملة : فترك المساعدة إن كان ممكناً . . فقد تعذَّر الفعل ، وبطل معنى القدرة ، والمساعدة إن كانت واجبة . . صار الذي لا بد له مضطراً لا قدرة له
The second division: that it be said: one of them has power over atoms, and the other over accidents, and they are different; so power over one does not entail power over the other. And this is impossible; because the accident is not independent of the substance, and the substance is not independent of the accident; so the act of each one is dependent on the other. So when the creator of accidents wants to create an accident, how can he create it when perhaps the creator of substances does not assist him in creating the substance at the time of his desire to create the accident? So he remains incapable and perplexed, and the incapable is not powerful. Likewise the creator of substances: if he wants to create a substance, perhaps the creator of accidents opposes him; so the other is prevented from creating the substance, leading to mutual hindrance. If it is said: whenever one of them wants to create a substance, the other assists him with the accident, and vice versa, we say: this assistance: is it obligatory, such that its opposite is inconceivable in reason, or [optional]? If you make it obligatory, that is arbitrary, rather it also nullifies power; for the creation of the substance by one compels the other to create the accident, and vice versa, so he has no power to refrain, so power is not realized with this. In sum: if refraining from assistance is possible, then the act is impossible, and the meaning of power is nullified; and if assistance is obligatory, then the one who cannot but [assist] is compelled, having no power.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against two gods: one has power over substances, the other over accidents. Ghazali first states this view and then refutes it. He argues that substances and accidents are interdependent; a substance always has accidents, and accidents always inhere in substances. Therefore, each creator's act depends on the other. For example, if the creator of accidents wants to create an accident, he needs a substance to inhere in, but if the creator of substances does not create that substance, he fails—showing him to be incapable. Similarly, the creator of substances needs accidents to accompany his substance, but the accident creator might not cooperate, leading to mutual hindrance (tamānu'). Then Ghazali considers an objection: what if they always assist each other? He asks whether this assistance is necessary or optional. If it is necessary, that is an arbitrary assumption and also nullifies power because then one creator's act compels the other, so neither has the power to refrain—hence no genuine power. If the assistance is optional and may be withheld, then the act becomes impossible, again nullifying power. Thus, in either case, the notion of two separate creators is incoherent.

Key Terms
جواهر
أعراض
تمانع
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that separate spheres of power avoid conflict between two gods.
Correction: Ghazali shows that because substances and accidents always coexist, each god's action requires the other's cooperation, leading to conflict or dependence.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say the act of each creator is dependent on the other?
A) Because they have equal power.
B) Because substances and accidents cannot exist separately; each requires the other.
C) Because the creators are friends and help each other.
D) Because accidents are more important.

Key Takeaway
Substances and accidents are interdependent, so two gods with separate domains would each be powerless without the other's cooperation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that different areas of your life (like career, family, worship) are under separate powers or controls, what does this lesson reveal about the impossibility of such division? How does that challenge you to submit every sphere to God alone?
?
In what ways do you act as if some part of your life is independent of God's control? What would it mean for you today to genuinely acknowledge His sole power over all things, even the things you feel are 'yours'?
?
The lesson shows that two powers lead to mutual hindrance and dependency. Reflect on a time you tried to rely on multiple sources for security or support. How did that create conflict or dependence? How can you turn to God alone as the ultimate provider?

The Objection of Mutual Cooperation Refuted
Ghazali refutes the objection of mutual cooperation by analyzing its two logical possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the idea that two gods could always cooperate, showing it leads to compulsion or impossibility.

Core Idea
Necessary cooperation is as bad as no cooperation—it nullifies power.

Ghazali now considers a possible objection: what if the two gods always assist each other? He examines this scenario carefully, asking whether this assistance is necessary or optional. Either way, he concludes, divine power is nullified. This deepens his refutation of dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage where Ghazali imagines the two gods always helping each other, and his two-pronged response.
Notice the dilemma structure.
2
Analyze the two possibilities
Ghazali presents two cases: assistance is either necessary (واجبة) or optional (تركها ممکن). Understand each case and its consequence.
3
Understand the conclusion
In both cases, Ghazali shows that the concept of power is undermined—either because one creator compels the other (no freedom) or because the act may fail (no capability).
This completes the refutation.

Source Text

Refutation of Two Gods as Creators of Good and Evil
Ghazali refutes the idea that one god creates good and another evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the idea of two separate creators for good and evil is rationally impossible, based on the nature of good and evil as relational rather than intrinsic.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not intrinsic; they depend on context, so power over one is power over all.

Ghazali presents a powerful rational argument against the notion that one god creates good and another creates evil. He shows that the same act can be good or evil depending on circumstances, so power over such acts cannot be divided. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own argumentative rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify Ghazali's key point
Notice that Ghazali immediately calls the objection 'nonsense' because evil is not evil in itself (laysa sharran li-dhatihi). This is the foundation of his refutation.
Evil is relational, not essential.
3
Examine the burning example
Ghazali gives the example of burning a Muslim's body (evil) vs. burning an unbeliever's body (good). The same act of burning becomes good or evil based on the person's belief. This shows the act itself is neutral.
4
Understand the implication for power
Since burnings are similar (mutamathila), power over one burning implies power over all. If one god has power over evil burnings, he must also have power over good burnings, leading to conflict (tamānu').
This contradiction proves the impossibility of two gods.
5
Connect to the Quranic verse
Ghazali concludes by citing Quran 21:22, which states that if there were multiple gods, both heavens and earth would be corrupted. The rational argument aligns with revelation.
The Quranic verse is the ultimate confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيكونُ أحدهما خالق الشر ، والآخر خالق الخير . أحدهما يخلق الخير قلنا : هذا هوس ؛ لأن الشرَّ ليس شراً لذاته ، بل هو من حيث مساو للخير ومماثل له ، والقدرة على الشيء قدرة على مثله ؛ فإنَّ إحراق بدن المسلم بالنار شر وإحراق بدن الكافر خيرٌ ودفع شر ، والشخص الواحد إذا تكلم بكلمة الإسلام .. انقلب الإحراق في حقه شراً ؛ فالقادر على إحراق لحمِهِ بالنار عند سكوته عن كلمة الإيمان .. لا بدَّ وأن يقدر على إحراقه عند النطق بها ؛ لأن نطقه بها صوت ينقضي ، لا يغيرُ ذات اللحم ، ولا ذاتَ النار ، ولا ذات الإحراق ، ولا يقلبُ جنساً ؛ فتكون الإحراقات متماثلة فيجب تعلق القدرة بالكل ؛ ويقتضي ذلك تمانعاً وتزاحماً وعلى الجملة : كيفما فُرضَ الأمرُ .. تولَّدَ منه اضطراب وفساد ، وهو الذي أراده الله تعالى بقوله : ( لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَنَا فلا مزيد على بیان القرآن
If it is said: then one of them would be the creator of evil, and the other the creator of good. One of them creates good, and the other evil. We say: this is nonsense; because evil is not evil in itself, but rather it is equal and similar to good. And power over a thing is power over its like. For burning the body of a Muslim with fire is evil, and burning the body of an unbeliever is good and a repulsion of evil. And the same person, if he speaks the word of Islam, the burning in his case becomes evil. So the one who is able to burn his flesh with fire when he is silent about the word of faith must necessarily be able to burn him when he utters it; because his utterance is a sound that passes away, it does not change the essence of the flesh, nor the essence of fire, nor the essence of burning, nor does it transform the genus; so the burnings are similar, so it is necessary that the power attaches to all; and that necessitates mutual hindrance and conflict. In sum: however the matter is supposed, confusion and corruption arise from it. And that is what Allah Almighty intended by His saying: 'If there were therein gods besides Allah, then verily both would have been corrupted.' So there is no addition beyond the explanation of the Quran.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that two gods could divide creation into good and evil. Ghazali responds that the objection is nonsense because evil is not an intrinsic quality; rather, acts are good or evil relative to circumstances. He gives an example: burning a Muslim's body is evil, but burning an unbeliever's body is good (as a punishment or defense). Moreover, the same person, if he is a Muslim, burning him is evil; if he becomes an unbeliever, burning him is good, and vice versa. The act of burning itself is similar in all cases; the change in the person's belief does not alter the physical essence. Power over one burning implies power over all similar burnings. Therefore, if one god had power over evil burnings, he must also have power over good burnings, leading to overlap and conflict (tamānu' and tazāhum). Ghazali then summarizes that any scenario of multiple gods leads to disorder, as the Quran has already declared (Surah Al-Anbiya 22).

شر
خير
قدرة
تماثل
تمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good and evil are distinct essences, each created by a different god.
Correction: Good and evil are relational; the same act can be good or evil depending on context, so power over such acts cannot be divided.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that a single god cannot be exclusively the creator of 'evil' acts?
A) Because evil acts are impossible for God.
B) Because the same act can be good or evil depending on context, so power over it is power over all instances.
C) Because evil is not created by God.
D) Because good and evil are different substances.

Key Takeaway
The idea of two separate creators for good and evil is rationally impossible because good and evil are not intrinsic qualities but contextual, and power over one implies power over all, leading to inevitable conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you previously labeled as purely 'evil' without considering context. How does Ghazali's argument that evil is not intrinsic challenge your judgment? Will you now pause to examine the circumstances before condemning someone's action?
?
This lesson shows that the same physical act can be good or evil depending on intention and situation. In your own life, are there habits or behaviors you consider neutral that could be either? Which ones might you be using in a way that turns them from good to evil?
?
If power over evil implies power over good, do you sometimes doubt God's power when you experience evil, assuming He could not bring good from it? How can you actively trust His all-encompassing power today in a situation that seems only evil?

Introduction to the Seven Essential Attributes of Allah
Ghazali introduces seven divine attributes and outlines the discussion plan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven essential attributes of Allah according to Ghazali and the two-part plan for discussing them.

Core Idea
The seven attributes are the foundation of all divine names and discussion.

Ghazali opens the second pole of his book by establishing the seven core attributes of Allah that must be affirmed. He then maps out a systematic approach: first examining each attribute individually, then looking at what they share. This passage sets the stage for the entire discussion on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a list of seven attributes followed by a two-part plan.
The footnote explains that these seven are the origin of other attributes.
2
Identify the seven attributes
From the list, identify each of the seven attributes in both Arabic and English. Write them down for reference.
They are: Knowing, Powerful, Living, Willing, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking.
3
Understand the two-part plan
Ghazali says the discussion branches into two: what is specific to each attribute, and what is common to all. The beginning will be with the first part: proving the existence of these attributes and explaining their specific rulings.
This structure guides the rest of the second pole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطب الثاني في الصفات وفيه سبع ؛ دعاوى ؛ إذ ندعي: أنَّه تعالى عالم ، قادر ، حي ، مريد ، سميع ، بصير ، متكلم ، فهذه سبع صفات (*) ، ويتشعب عنها نظر في أمرين : أحدهما : ما تختص به آحاد الصفات والثاني : ما تشترك فيه جميع الصفات فلتقع البداية بالقسم الأول ؛ وهو إثبات أصل الصفات ، وشرح خصوص أحكامها (*) هذه الصفات السبع هي أصل الصفات والأسماء الأخرى له سبحانه ، فلذا لزمت العناية بها ، وباقي الصفات - كالرحمة والرأفة والرزق واللطف - متفرع عنها وراجع إليها كما يلحظ المتأمل ، وقد أشار المصنف لذلك ( ص ٢١٧) .
The second pole on attributes. It contains seven claims; for we claim: He, exalted, is Knowing, Powerful, Living, Willing, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking. These are seven attributes. And from them branches consideration of two matters: first, what is specific to each attribute; second, what is common to all attributes. So let the beginning be with the first part, which is the affirmation of the source of the attributes and the explanation of their specific rulings. (*) These seven attributes are the source of the attributes and other names of Him, glory be to Him, so attention to them is necessary. The remaining attributes—like mercy, compassion, provision, and kindness—are derived from them and refer back to them, as the careful observer will note, and the author has indicated that (p. 217).
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage introduces the second pole of the book, which deals with God's attributes. Ghazali lists seven attributes: knowledge, power, life, will, hearing, sight, speech. He says these are seven claims (دعاوى) to be proven. Then he outlines that the discussion will cover two aspects: what is specific to each attribute, and what is common to all. He begins with the first aspect: proving the existence of these attributes and explaining their specific rulings. The footnote adds that these seven attributes are the origin of all other divine names and attributes like mercy and kindness, which are branches of these.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these seven attributes are the only attributes of Allah.
Correction: These seven are the foundational attributes; other attributes like mercy, compassion, provision, and kindness are derived from them.

Checkpoint
How many essential attributes does Ghazali list in the introduction to the second pole?
A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
These seven attributes are the foundation for all other divine names and attributes; understanding them is essential for discussing God's nature.

Introduction to the Seven Foundational Attributes
Ghazali enumerates seven attributes and outlines the discussion plan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطب الثاني
في الصفات
وفيه سبع دعاوى؛ إذ ندعي: أنَّه تعالى عالم ، قادر ، حي ، مريد ، سميع ، بصير ، متكلم ، فهذه سبع صفات (*) ، ويتشعب
عنها نظر في أمرين :
أحدهما : ما تختص به آحاد الصفات
والثاني : ما تشترك فيه جميع الصفات
فلتقع البداية بالقسم الأول ؛ وهو إثبات أصل الصفات ، وشرح
خصوص أحكامها
(*) هذه الصفات السبع هي أصل الصفات والأسماء الأخرى له سبحانه ، فلذا لزمت العناية بها ، وباقي الصفات - كالرحمة والرأفة والرزق واللطف - متفرع عنها وراجع إليها كما يلحظ المتأمل ، وقد أشار المصنف لذلك ( ص ٢١٧ ) .
The second pole (al-quṭb al-thānī) on the attributes (al-ṣifāt). It contains seven claims: we claim that He, exalted, is Knowing (‘ālim), Powerful (qādir), Living (ḥayy), Willing (murīd), Hearing (samī‘), Seeing (baṣīr), Speaking (mutakallim). So these are seven attributes. And from them branch out consideration in two matters: One of them: what is specific to individual attributes, and the second: what is shared among all attributes. So let the beginning be with the first part: which is the establishment of the basis of the attributes and the explanation of their specific rulings. (*) These seven attributes are the basis of the attributes and the other names of Him, Exalted, therefore attention to them is necessary, and the rest of the attributes – such as mercy, compassion, provision, kindness – are branched from them and return to them, as the observer notices, and the author has indicated that (p. 217).
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major section (قطب) of the book, which focuses on the divine attributes. Al-Ghazali enumerates seven core attributes: knowledge, power, life, will, hearing, sight, and speech. These are considered the foundational attributes (أصل الصفات). The author then outlines the plan: first, to establish these attributes and discuss what is specific to each; second, to discuss what is common among all attributes. The footnote (likely an editor's note) emphasizes that these seven are the basis, and other attributes like mercy, etc., derive from them. This passage serves as an overview and roadmap for the subsequent discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that seven attributes are the roots of all others. Which of these seven attributes do you find most difficult to truly believe in with your heart, not just your mind? What would change in your life if you lived as if that attribute were fully real right now?
?
Al-Ghazali says the other attributes (like mercy, kindness) branch from these seven. Take a moment: when was the last time you felt God's mercy? Can you trace that feeling back to one of the seven foundational attributes? If not, does that reveal a gap in your understanding of God?
?
This passage presents a roadmap for studying God's attributes. But knowing about God is not the same as knowing God. Between merely reading these words and truly encountering the Living, Knowing, Hearing God—what stands in your way? What step will you take today to cross that gap?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
The Order of the World as Proof of God's Power
Ghazali presents the claim and syllogism, then defends the first premise with sensory evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the world's observable order and precision to establish that its Originator is powerful.

Core Idea
The world's intricate order is sensory proof that its Maker is powerful.

This lesson focuses on Ghazali's foundational claim: the world is a well-ordered act, and this order points to a powerful agent. He presents a clear syllogism and defends its first premise—that the world is indeed well-ordered—using everyday observation of the human body and nature. You will see how sensory evidence forms the first pillar of his proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully along with the literal English translation. Pay attention to the structure: claim, syllogism, and the first objection and response.
Notice that Ghazali uses a formal syllogistic style.
2
Identify the two premises
The syllogism has two premises: (1) Every well-ordered act comes from a powerful agent. (2) The world is a well-ordered act. Identify where each is stated.
3
Understand the objection about the first premise
The objector questions: 'Why is the world well-ordered?' Ghazali answers by defining 'well-ordered' as arrangement and proportion, and points to the human body as evidence known by sense.
This shows that the first premise is grounded in sensory observation.
4
Reflect on the sensory evidence
Think about the wonders of the human body Ghazali alludes to. How does this make the denial of the world's order impossible?
This lesson does not cover the second premise; that comes next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الأولى القدرة
ندعي: أنَّ محدِثَ العالم قادرٌ؛ لأن العالم فعل محكم، مرتب، متقن، منظوم، مشتمل على أنواع من العجائب والآيات؛ وذلك يدل على القدرة.
ونرتب القياس فنقول: كلُّ فعل محكم فهو صادر من فاعل قادر، والعالم فعل محكم؛ فهو إذن صادر من فاعل قادر.
ففي أي الأصلين النزاع؟
فإن قيل: لم قلتم: إن العالم فعل محكم؟
قلنا: عنينا بكونه محكماً: ترتبه، وانتظامه، وتناسبَه؛ فمن نظر في أعضاء نفسه الظاهرة والباطنة ظهر له من عجائب الإتقان ما يطول حصره، فهذا أصل يُدرِكُ معرفته الحس والمشاهدة، ولا يتسع جحده.
تحريجة: ما دليل إحكام العالم؟
فإن قيل: فيمَ يُدرك الأصلُ الآخر؛ وهو أن كل فعل محكم مرتب ففاعله قادر؟
قلنا: هذا مدركُهُ ضرورةُ العقل؛ فالعقل يصدقُ به بغير دليل.
ولا يقدر العاقل على جحده، ولكنا مع هذا نجرِّدُ دليلاً يقطع دابر الجحود والعناد. فنقول:
نعني بكونه قادراً: أن الفعل الصادر منه لا يخلو: إما أن يصدر عنه لذاته، أو لمعنى زائد عليه، وباطل أن يقال: صدر عنه لذاته؛ إذ لو كان كذلك لكان قديماً، فدل على أنَّه صدر لزائد على ذاته، فالصفة الزائدة التي بها تهيَّأ الفعلُ للوجود نسميها: قدرة.
The first attribute: power.
We claim: that the originator of the world is powerful, because the world is a well-ordered act, arranged, precise, organized, containing types of wonders and signs; and that indicates power.
And we arrange the syllogism and say: every well-ordered act proceeds from a powerful agent, and the world is a well-ordered act; therefore it proceeds from a powerful agent.
So in which of the two premises is the dispute?
If it is said: why do you say that the world is a well-ordered act?
We say: we mean by its being well-ordered: its arrangement, its order, and its proportion; so whoever examines the organs of his own body, external and internal, there appear to him wonders of precision whose enumeration would be lengthy. So this is a principle whose knowledge is grasped by sense and observation, and its denial is not possible.
[Marginal note: what is the proof of the world's precision?]
If it is said: how is the other principle known—that every well-ordered, arranged act has a powerful agent?
We say: this is grasped by the necessity of reason; for reason affirms it without proof.
And the rational person is not able to deny it, but despite this we extract a proof that cuts off the root of denial and stubbornness. So we say:
We mean by His being powerful: that the act proceeding from Him does not escape either that it proceeds from His essence or from an extra meaning; and it is false to say that it proceeds from His essence, for if that were so, it would be eternal. So it indicates that it proceeds from something additional to His essence. So the additional attribute by which the act becomes prepared for existence we call: power.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's power (qudra) based on the order and precision of the world. It begins by stating the claim: the originator of the world is powerful because the world is a well-ordered act (fi'l muhkam), arranged, precise, and full of wonders. This is presented as a syllogism: every well-ordered act comes from a powerful agent; the world is a well-ordered act; therefore, the world comes from a powerful agent.

Ghazali then addresses potential objections. First, an objector asks: 'Why do you claim the world is well-ordered?' The answer: 'Well-ordered' means its arrangement, order, and proportion. This is known through sensory observation of the human body's organs, both external and internal, which display such precision that listing all wonders would be lengthy. This principle is grasped by sense and observation, and denial is impossible.

Second, an objector asks: 'How do you know that every well-ordered act implies a powerful agent?' Ghazali responds that this is known by necessity of reason (darurat al-'aql); reason affirms it without need for proof. No rational person can deny it. Nevertheless, he provides an additional rational argument to eliminate stubbornness: He defines 'powerful' (qadir) as meaning that the act does not proceed from the agent's essence alone, because if it did, the act would be eternal (since the essence is eternal). Therefore, the act must proceed from an extra attribute (sifa za'ida) beyond the essence, and that attribute is called 'power' (qudra). This further clarifies that the world's contingency (as a temporal act) points to a power in the agent.

Throughout, Ghazali emphasizes that the argument combines sensory evidence (for the world's order) and rational necessity (that order implies power), with a supplementary definitional argument to solidify the concept of power.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the world's order is a matter of opinion or faith, not empirical fact.
Correction: Ghazali insists that the order and precision of the world—especially in the human body—are known by sense and observation, making denial unreasonable.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how is the well-ordered nature of the world known?
A) Through rational deduction alone
B) Through sensory observation and experience
C) Through scriptural revelation
D) Through the consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The world's observable order provides undeniable sensory evidence that its Creator is powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the precision of your own body is an undeniable proof of God's power. When was the last time you stopped to truly examine your own hand or your heartbeat and felt awe? If you cannot remember, then you have been treating the greatest sign as ordinary. Will you commit today to spending five minutes in silent reflection on the design of your body, allowing it to awaken your awareness of the Creator?
?
This lesson says denial of the world's order is not possible for one who observes. Yet, do you sometimes live as if your existence is random? The proof is right before your eyes—in every cell of your being. How will you let this undeniable reality transform your gratitude and your relationship with Allah, starting now?
?
If the order of the world is sensory proof of God's power, then every time you see disorder in your life, what does that mean? Could it be that your perception of disorder is merely a failure to see the deeper wisdom? Ask yourself: do you truly trust that the One who arranged your body with such precision is also arranging your life with wisdom, even when you do not understand?
